Definition of a Minor Sin and How It Becomes Major
The author defines a minor sin and lists four conditions that turn it into a major sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a minor sin is defined and the four conditions that can elevate it to a major sin.

Core Idea
Minor sins become major through persistence, negligence, pride, or being a sinful role model.

This passage provides a clear rule for distinguishing minor sins from major ones, but more importantly, it warns that minor sins are not static in their gravity. Factors like persistence and public example can transform a small infraction into a serious matter. Let's examine the text to grasp both the definition and the transformative factors.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise style and the important terms that define the minor sin and its escalation.
The passage begins with a heading in brackets; consider it part of the unit.
2
Identify the definition of a minor sin
Find where the author defines what counts as a minor sin. Note that it is a negative definition: whatever does not meet the definition of a major sin.
The definition is broad and open-ended.
3
List the four factors that make a minor sin major
The author enumerates four causes that transform a minor sin into a major sin. List them in order and note the key Arabic terms.
These factors are: persistence, negligence, pride, and being a followed scholar.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about how these factors apply in practice. Why would committing a sin while being a role model make it more severe? Why does persistence matter?
This connects to the broader theme of accountability in Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ ضابط المعصية الصغيرة، وأنها قد تنقلب كبيرةً] وكل ما خرج عن حد الكبيرة وضَابِطهَا فهو صغيرة، ولا تنحصر أفرادها ، وقد تنقلب الصَّغيرة كبيرة؛ بالإصرار عليها، والتهاون والفرح والافتخار بها، وصدورها من عالم يُقتدى به فيها.
[The rule of the minor sin, and that it may turn into a major sin] And everything that departs from the limit and definition of a major sin is a minor sin, and its individual instances are not confined. And the minor sin may turn into a major sin by persistence upon it, negligence, happiness and pride in it, and its issuance from a scholar who is followed in it.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage defines a minor sin and explains how it can acquire the ruling of a major sin. It first provides a negative definition: any sin that does not meet the criteria of a major sin is a minor sin. It notes that minor sins are not limited in number. Then it lists four causes that can transform a minor sin into a major sin: persistence (الإصرار), treating it lightly (التهاون), feeling joy and pride in it (الفرح والافتخار), and committing it when one is a scholar who is followed (يُقتدى به). The passage thus gives a rule for when minor sins escalate in gravity.

Key Terms
ضابط
المعصية الصغيرة
الكبيرة
الإصرار
التهاون
الافتخار
عالم يُقتدى به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a sin is either always minor or always major by its essence, regardless of context.
Correction: This passage shows that external factors such as persistence, negligence, pride, and social influence can change the ruling of a sin from minor to major.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition that turns a minor sin into a major sin according to the passage?
A) Persistence upon the sin
B) Negligence and taking it lightly
C) Committing it once without repentance
D) Being a scholar who is followed

Key Takeaway
A minor sin can become major if it is done persistently, lightly, boastfully, or by a person of influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a minor sin you have been persisting in, thinking it is too small to matter? What will you do today to break that pattern and seek forgiveness?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel a secret pride or a sense of achievement? How can you train yourself to feel genuine shame instead, as this lesson demands?
?
If you hold a position of influence—as a parent, teacher, or friend—are you aware that your minor sins can lead others astray? What specific step will you take this week to set a better example?

Immediate Repentance from Major Sins
A ruling on the obligation of immediate repentance from major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that repentance from major sins, including disbelief, is an immediate personal obligation upon committing the sin.

Core Idea
Repentance from major sins must be done instantly without delay.

This lesson builds on the division of sins into minor and major. The author now establishes a critical ruling: repentance from major sins is not something to postpone. As soon as one commits a major sin, repentance becomes an individual duty that must be performed at that very moment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's own wording come through before you interpret.
Note the sharp phrasing: 'فورًا' (immediately).
2
Understand the ruling's conditions
The ruling applies to major sins, including disbelief. It is a personal obligation (واجب عين), meaning every sinner is individually responsible.
3
Reflect on the immediacy
The phrase 'في الحال' means at the time of the sin, without any delay. The word 'فورًا' reinforces this urgency.
Compare with the possibility of postponement – the author rules it out.
4
Consider the implication
No delay is permissible. Even a small gap between sin and repentance is not allowed for major sins.
This is a strict ruling emphasizing the seriousness of major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ وجوب التّوبة في الحال] فَالثَّاني) ، أي : وإذا علمت انقسامَ الذُّنوب إلى صغائر وكبائر فاعلم أنَّ الكبائر الشَّامِلة للكفر (مِنْهُ المَتَابُ وَاجِبٌ) عَيْنًا ( في الحَالِ) أي: حالَ التَّلبس بالمعصية فورًا
[The obligation of repentance immediately] The second, that is: when you know the division of sins into minor and major, know that the major sins inclusive of disbelief, repentance from it is personally obligatory immediately, i.e., at the time of committing the sin, instantly.
Source pages p. 589

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the timing of repentance. It follows the earlier division of sins and focuses on major sins, including disbelief. It states that repentance from such sins is a personal obligation (واجب عين) that must be performed immediately (في الحال), meaning at the very moment of committing the sin, without any delay. The phrase 'عينا' emphasizes that it is an individual duty on every sinner. The passage thus establishes the imperative of immediate repentance.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance from major sins can be delayed or postponed without consequence.
Correction: The passage states that repentance from major sins is obligatory immediately (في الحال), meaning at the time of the sin, without delay.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is repentance from major sins obligatory?
A) Whenever one feels like it
B) After performing good deeds
C) Immediately upon committing the sin
D) Only after death

Key Takeaway
Repentance from major sins is an immediate personal obligation that must be performed at the moment of sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Is there a major sin you are holding onto, thinking you will repent later? What would it cost you to repent now, and what is the risk of delaying?

The Three Pillars of Repentance
Defines legal repentance and lists its three pillars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of legal repentance (al-tawba al-shar'iyya) and its three essential pillars: desisting, regret, and firm resolution.

Core Idea
True repentance rests on three pillars: stop, regret, resolve never to return.

This lesson examines the foundational definition of repentance in Islamic theology. The text establishes that repentance is not merely feeling sorry but requires concrete actions and a change of heart. We will explore the three pillars that make repentance valid according to the scholars.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that repentance is immediately obligatory upon committing a sin. The passage begins by affirming this obligation.
This establishes the urgency of repentance.
2
Learn about partial repentance
The author clarifies that repentance from some sins while persisting in others is valid, using the example of a disbeliever embracing Islam. This contradicts Abu Hashim.
Repentance does not require abandoning all sins at once.
3
Identify the three pillars
Read the definition of legal repentance and locate the three enumerated pillars: desisting, regret, and determination.
The text explicitly numbers them.
4
Grasp the significance of regret
Note that regret is called the greatest pillar because it implies the other two. Reflect on why this is central.
Regret is the inner turning of the heart.
5
Apply the general rule
Understand that general repentance suffices; you don't need to list every sin. Only one pillar (desisting) requires specific action in the moment.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب التوبة في الحال عليه (1) ، وقوله ((منه)» ، أي: من جميعه أو بعضه، بناءً على صحة التَّوبة عن بعض المعاصي (٢) مع الإصرار على البعض، ولو كان سر (۳) ، للإجماع على أنَّ الكافر إذا أسلم وتابَ عن كُفره مع استدامته على بعض المعاصي صحت (٤) توبته وإسلامه، ولم يُعاقب إلَّا عقوبة تلك المعصية، خلافًا لأبي هاشم والمُراد بالمَتَاب : التّوبَةُ الشَّرعيّةُ ؛ لأنَّها عند الإطلاق لا تنصرف إلَّا إليها ، وهي ، وهي ما استجمع ثلاثة أركان ١ - الإقلاع عن المعصية (٥) . فأربعة ذنوب، الذنب الأول ، وتأخير توبته في اللحظة الأولى، وتأخير التوبة من هذين في الثانية ، وثالثة فثمانية وهكذا ... أفاده المصنف . الأمير . قسم السمعيات ۲ - والندم على فعلها (١) ، وهو رُكنها الأعظم (۲) ـ والعزم على أن لا يعود إلى مثلها أبدا عزماً جازماً . كلها فإذا حصلت هذه الشروط صحتْ التَّوبة ولو من المعاصي إجمالا (۳) ولو علمها تفصيلا، وإنْ فُقد أحدها لم تَصِحَّ،
The obligation of repentance immediately upon it (1), and his saying 'from it' meaning: from all of it or part of it, based on the validity of repentance from some sins (2) while persisting in others, even if it is major (3), due to consensus that when a disbeliever embraces Islam and repents from his disbelief while continuing in some sins, his repentance and Islam are valid (4), and he is not punished except the punishment of that sin, contrary to Abu Hashim. And what is meant by al-matāb (the act of repentance) is al-tawba al-shar'iyya (the legal repentance); because when used absolutely it refers only to it, and it is that which comprises three pillars: 1 - Desisting from the sin (5). So four sins: the first sin, delaying his repentance in the first moment, delaying repentance from these two in the second moment, and a third becomes eight, and so on... The author stated it. Al-Amir. The section of sam'iyyāt (heard matters). 2 - Regret for having done it (1), and it is its greatest pillar (2) — and the determination not to return to its like ever, with a firm determination. All of them: when these conditions are met, repentance is valid even if from sins in general (3) and even if he knows them in detail, and if any one of them is missing, it is not valid.
Source pages p. 590

Commentary
This passage defines the legal repentance (al-tawba al-shar'iyya) and states its obligation immediately upon committing a sin. The author first clarifies that repentance can be from some sins while persisting in others, using the example of a disbeliever who repents from disbelief but continues other sins — his repentance is still valid, and he is only punished for those persisting sins. This is contrary to the Mu'tazilite Abu Hashim. Then the definition is given: repentance in the legal sense is a term that requires three pillars (arkān). The first pillar is desisting (al-iqlā') from the sin if one is currently engaged in it. The text then includes a note from the commentator al-Amir that delaying repentance multiplies sins exponentially. After a section heading for sam'iyyāt (heard matters, i.e., revealed knowledge), the second pillar is regret (al-nadam) over the sin, which is called the greatest pillar because it implies the others. The third pillar is a firm determination (al-'azm) never to return to the sin. If all three conditions are present, repentance is valid even if one repents from sins only generally without specifying each one, and if any condition is missing, repentance is invalid.

Key Terms
توبة
إقلاع
ندم
عزم
أركان
سمعيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think repentance requires listing every individual sin explicitly.
Correction: The text clarifies that general repentance suffices; you do not need to enumerate each sin.

Checkpoint
What are the three pillars of repentance according to this passage?
A) Desisting, regret, and determination not to return
B) Confession, regret, and restitution
C) Abandoning, hiding, and apologizing
D) Fasting, prayer, and charity

Key Takeaway
Repentance is valid only when it includes desisting from the sin, regret for having done it, and a firm resolve not to return.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When do you delay repentance? The passage mentions that delaying multiplies sins. Reflect on a specific sin you have been delaying repentance for. What prevents you from stopping now?
?
The passage says regret is the greatest pillar. Do you truly regret your past sins in the depth of your heart, or do you merely fear consequences? How can you cultivate genuine regret?
?
Repentance requires a firm resolve never to return. Is there a sin you have repented from but still feel attached to? What does that say about your resolve? Will you renew your repentance with a stronger determination?

Conditions of Tawbah and Its Obligation
Al-Shanwani enumerates conditions of tawbah and states its obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four conditions of valid tawbah and the basis of its obligation according to the Ash'ari school.

Core Idea
True repentance requires regret, cessation, resolve, and when necessary, restoring others' rights.

Al-Shanwani outlines the essential components of tawbah. Before we can judge its validity, we need to know its conditions. This passage lays out the three internal conditions, adds a fourth for interpersonal sins, and clarifies the debate over whether obligation is known by reason or revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three core conditions
Read the passage and pick out the three conditions: regret (al-nadam), ceasing the sin (al-iqla'), and firm resolve (al-'azm). Note that regret is called the greatest pillar.
The term 'iqla' is implied by context; the explicit wording is 'regret' and 'resolve'.
2
Understand the fourth condition for interpersonal sins
Notice that for sins involving another person's rights, a fourth condition is added: either restoring the injustice or obtaining the person's forgiveness.
This distinguishes between sins against Allah and sins against people.
3
Examine the obligation and its proof
The passage states tawbah is individually obligatory by consensus. The dispute is over the evidence: Ash'aris say it is known through scripture (sam'), Mu'tazila claim reason ('aql).
This reflects a key methodological difference in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات ۲ - والندم على فعلها ، وهو ركنها الأعظم ـ والعزم على أن لا يعود إلى مثلها أبدا عزماً جازماً . كلها فإذا حصلت هذه الشروط صحتْ التَّوبة ولو من المعاصي إجمالا ولو علمها تفصيلا، وإنْ فُقد أحدها لم تَصِحَّ، وهذا إذا كانت المعصية بين العبد وبين الله تعالى لا تتعلق بحق آدمي، أمَّا المُتعلّقة بالآدمي فلها شرطٌ رابع ، وهو : رَدُّ الظُّلامَة إلى صاحبها، أو تحصيل البراءة منه. ولا خلاف في وجوبها عينًا ، إِنَّما النّزاعُ في دليل الوجوب ؛ فعندنا هو السَّمع كقوله : ﴿وَتُوبُوا إِلَى اللَّهِ جَمِيعًا أَيُّهَ الْمُؤْمِنُونَ ﴾ [النور: ۳۱] وعند المعتزلة العقل.
Section of Sam'iyyat. 2 - And regret for the act, and it is its greatest pillar. And the resolve to never return to the like of it, a firm resolve. All of them. When these conditions are obtained, tawbah is valid even if from sins in general, and even if he knows them in detail, and if any one of them is absent, it is not valid. This is when the sin is between the servant and Allah Most High, not involving a human right. As for that which involves a human, it has a fourth condition: restoring the injustice to its owner, or obtaining release from him. And there is no disagreement in its individual obligation; rather the dispute is concerning the proof of obligation. According to us it is scripture, as His saying: 'And turn to Allah all together, O believers!' (al-Nur 31). According to the Mu'tazila it is reason.
Source pages p. 591

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions of tawbah (repentance) and discusses its obligation. The author first lists the three essential conditions: (1) al-nadam (regret), described as the greatest pillar, (2) al-iqla' (ceasing the sin), implied by the context, and (3) al-‘azm (firm resolve) never to return to the sin. He then states that if these conditions are met, tawbah is valid even if the penitent only repents generally without specifying every sin. Next, he adds a fourth condition for sins that violate the rights of another person: restoring the wronged right or obtaining the person’s forgiveness. Following this, he affirms that tawbah is individually obligatory (wajib ‘ayn) by consensus; the only dispute is over the evidence: Ash‘aris argue it is known through scripture (sam‘), citing the Quranic verse, while the Mu‘tazila claim reason (aql) suffices. This underscores the Ash‘ari principle that moral obligations are derived from revelation.

Key Terms
التوبة
الندم
الإقلاع
العزم
رد الظلامة
وجوب
السمع
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawbah requires enumerating every sin individually.
Correction: The passage clarifies that tawbah is valid even if one repents generally from sins, as long as the three conditions are met.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth condition of tawbah when the sin involves another person?
A) Ceasing the sin
B) Regret
C) Resolve not to return
D) Restoring rights or seeking release

Key Takeaway
Tawbah has three core conditions (regret, cessation, resolve) plus a fourth when others' rights are involved, and its obligation is proven by scripture according to the Ash'ari school.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you feel deep regret as the greatest pillar of tawbah, or do you just quickly ask forgiveness and move on? What sin today can you truly regret right now and resolve to leave?
?
If your sin has wronged someone else, have you taken the step to restore their right or seek their forgiveness? What is holding you back from doing that this week?
?
The passage says tawbah is obligatory on every believer—no exceptions. Is there a sin you have been avoiding repenting from because you think it is too big or you are too ashamed? Will you turn to Allah tonight with a sincere heart?

Divine Grace and the Irrevocability of Repentance
Explains forgiveness of major sins by divine grace and refutes Mu'tazila on repentance revocation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that major sins can be forgiven by Allah's pure grace without repentance, and that sincere repentance is not invalidated by later relapse; a relapse is a new sin requiring new repentance.

Core Idea
Repentance once accepted is never revoked by a future sin; each sin stands alone.

This lesson explores a key doctrinal point from ‘Aqidat al-Jawhara: the relationship between repentance, forgiveness, and relapse. The author explains that Allah’s grace can forgive major sins independently of repentance, and that the Mu'tazila insistence on repentance revocation is mistaken. Understanding this clarifies the balance between divine mercy and human accountability in Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a statement about forgiveness, then a hadith, then a refutation of the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the two key claims
Find the claim that major sins may be forgiven by pure grace and lightened by acts of obedience. Then identify the claim that repentance is not invalidated if one returns to sin.
These two points are the core of the passage.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila held that valid repentance requires never repeating the sin; otherwise the repentance is nullified and past sins return. Contrast this with the Sunni position presented here.
The commentator explicitly refutes this view.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider that each sin is an individual act; repentance deals with past sins, and a future sin is a separate violation requiring its own repentance. This highlights Allah's mercy and the importance of continuous repentance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تجديد التوبة يتجدد الذنب المصنف رحمه الله تعالى ما يفيد توقف غفران الكبائر على التوبة؛ فقد تغفر بالفضل المحض، وقد يخفف منها بالطاعات، وفي حديث أنس قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إذا تاب العبد أنسى الله الحفظة ذنوبه» خرجه ابن عساكر. ولما ذهب المعتزلة إلى أن من شروط صحة التوبة أن لا يعاود الذنب بعد التوبة، فإن عاوده انتقضت توبته وعادت ذنوبه.. رد عليهم بقوله: (ولا انتقاض) لتوبة التائب الشرعية (إن يعد للحال) أي: إن للحالة الأولى التي كان عليها من التلبس بالذنوب، ولا تعود ذنوبه التي تاب منها عليه، بل عوده ونقضه معصية أخرى، يجب عليه أن يجدد منها توبة أخرى.
Renewal of repentance upon renewal of sin. The author, may Allah have mercy on him, indicates that the forgiveness of major sins is not dependent on repentance; for they may be forgiven by pure grace, and some of them may be lightened by acts of obedience. And in the hadith of Anas, he said: The Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم said: 'When the servant repents, Allah makes the guardians forget his sins.' It was recorded by Ibn Asakir. And when the Mu'tazila held that among the conditions of valid repentance is that he does not return to the sin after repentance, for if he returns his repentance is invalidated and his sins return... he refuted them by saying: (There is no invalidation) of the lawful repentance of the penitent (if he returns to the state), i.e., if he returns to the first state which he was upon, of being occupied with sins, and his sins which he repented from do not return upon him; rather his return and his violation is another sin, for which he must renew a repentance.
Source pages p. 592

Commentary
This passage is a combination of explanation and refutation. It begins by stating that forgiveness of major sins is not necessarily contingent upon repentance; Allah may forgive them by pure grace or mitigate them through righteous deeds. A hadith is cited to illustrate that repentance leads to forgiveness. The commentator then refutes the Mu'tazila claim that returning to a sin invalidates one's previous repentance. He argues that the original repentance remains intact; the relapse is a new sin that necessitates a separate repentance. The argument follows the order: clarification, evidence, then refutation. The key nuance is that repentance is not nullified by subsequent sin; each sin demands its own repentance.

Key Terms
توبة
غفران
كبائر
فضل محض
معتزلة
انتقاض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If you repent then sin again, your repentance is void and previous sins return.
Correction: Repentance remains valid; the new sin is a separate offense requiring its own repentance. Past sins are not reinstated.

Checkpoint
What happens to a person's previous repentance if he commits the same sin again, according to this passage?
A) It is invalidated and must be repeated.
B) It remains valid, but the new sin requires a new repentance.
C) It becomes insufficient and must be repeated from the beginning.
D) It is partially accepted only if he repents again.

Key Takeaway
Major sins can be forgiven by Allah's grace, and sincere repentance is never undone by later sins—each sin requires its own repentance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted that your repentance was accepted because you later fell into the same sin? How does this lesson reshape your view of Allah's mercy when you stumble again? Will you now thank Him for the past forgiveness and renew your repentance without despair?
?
The author says major sins may be forgiven by pure grace without repentance. Does that make you take sin lightly or fill you with awe? What will you do today to show gratitude for that grace?
?
When you commit a repeated sin, do you treat yourself as if your old slate is wiped clean or as if you've failed completely? Starting now, will you treat each fall as a new sin requiring a new turn to Allah, not as a collapse of your entire faith?

Scholarly Disagreement on the Proof for Acceptance of Repentance
The commentator explains the scholarly difference on the acceptance of repentance by definitive or speculative proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on whether Allah is obligated to accept repentance and the scholarly disagreement on whether the proof for acceptance is definitive or speculative.

Core Idea
Allah is not rationally obligated to accept repentance; the obligation is only by promise, and scholars differ on the certainty of that proof.

This lesson addresses a key issue in Islamic theology: the status of repentance. The commentator presents the Sunni position that Allah is not rationally obligated to accept repentance, and then explains the disagreement among scholars about whether the proof for acceptance is definitive or merely speculative. A special case is the repentance of the disbeliever, which is definitively accepted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the question being asked: whether acceptance of repentance is obligatory by divine law and promise.
The passage begins with a general section on Sam'iyyat.
2
Identify the two scholarly positions
Find the views of Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi versus Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. Note the type of proof each relies on: speculative (zanni) or definitive (qat'i).
Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi say the proof is speculative; Imam al-Ash'ari says it is definitive.
3
Understand the exception for the disbeliever
Notice that the repentance of the disbeliever is stated to be definitively accepted by divine law. Consider why this might be an exception.
The passage cuts off before completing the Quranic citation.

Section on Sam'iyyat. But he renews repentance for what he committed, i.e., for the sin he committed a second time. So the people of truth from Ahl al-Sunnah said: Allah is not obligated rationally to accept the repentance of the penitent; rather, nothing is obligatory upon Him, exalted, at all. And our Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: Rather, by a definitive proof. And it is known from the poem that the repentance of the disbeliever is definitively accepted by divine law; because of His saying...

Commentary
This passage is a classification of a scholarly disagreement. It first presents the Sunni position: Allah is not rationally obligated to accept repentance; nothing is obligatory upon Him absolutely. Then it poses the question of whether acceptance is obligatory by divine law and promise (sam'an wa wa'dan). Two positions are given: Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi consider it obligatory but by a speculative (zanni) proof, while Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari holds it is by a definitive (qat'i) proof. Finally, the commentator notes that from the poem it is understood that the repentance of the disbeliever is definitively accepted by divine law, likely citing a Quranic proof (cut off). The argument proceeds from general principle to specific disagreement, then to the exception for the disbeliever.

D) Because the disbeliever's repentance is accepted anyway.

?
The scholars differed on the certainty of the proof for acceptance. Is your own hope in Allah's mercy built on a firm certainty or on a 'speculative' wish? What practical steps can you take today to strengthen that certainty through the revealed texts?

The Rational and Scriptural Status of Repentance
Sanusi discusses the rational and scriptural basis for acceptance of repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological debate on whether God is obliged to accept repentance, and the difference between a probable (dhanni) and categorical (qati') proof in this context.

Core Idea
God is not rationally obliged to accept repentance; scriptural obligation is debated.

This lesson examines a foundational issue in Islamic theology: the nature of God's acceptance of repentance. The author first establishes that nothing is rationally obligatory upon God, then presents the scholarly disagreement on whether His promise to accept repentance is binding by scripture, and whether the proof for that is probable or categorical.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial statement on rational non-obligation
Notice that Ahl al-Sunnah affirm that God is not rationally obliged to accept repentance. This sets the stage for the debate.
This is a key Ash'ari principle: nothing is obligatory upon God by reason.
2
Understand the two views on scriptural obligation
Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi say it is obligatory by a probable (dhanni) proof; Imam al-Ash'ari says by a categorical (qati') proof. Reflect on why this difference matters.
The type of proof affects the certainty of the obligation.
3
Grasp the distinction between dhanni and qati'
Dhanni means probable, open to interpretation; qati' means definitive, unambiguous. This distinction is crucial in usul al-fiqh and kalam.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
If the proof is only dhanni, then the obligation is not absolutely certain; if qati', it is a matter of faith.
This connects to broader discussions on God's attributes and promises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات لكن يجدد توبة لما اقترف وفي القبول رأبهم قد اختلف لكن يجدّد توبة لما اقترف، أي للذنب الذي ارتكبه ثانيا، وفي طريق القبول للتوبة وكيفيته رأيهم يعني العلماء قد اختلف، فقال أهل الحق من أهل السنة: لا يجب على الله عقلاً قبول توبة التائب، بل لا يجب عليه تعالى شيء مطلقاً، وهل يجب قبولها سمعاً ووعداً؟ فقال إمام الحرمين والقاضي: نعم، لكن بدليل ظني، إذ لم يثبت في ذلك نص قاطع لا يحتمل التأويل، وقال إمامنا أبو الحسن الأشعري: بل بدليل قطعي. وقد علم من النظم أن توبة الكافر مقطوع بقبولها سمعاً؛ لقوله تعالى: {قل للذين كفروا إن ينتهوا يغفر لهم ما قد سلف} [الأنفال: 38]. وتوبة المؤمن العاصي فيها قولان: أحدهما المشهور يقول: بقبولها قطعاً، والآخر الأصح يقول: بقبولها ظناً، وشرط صحتها: صدورها قبل الغرغرة، وقبل طلوع الشمس من مغربها. قال النووي رحمه الله تعالى: ففي حال الغرغرة – وهي حالة النزع – لا تقبل توبة ولا غيرها. كما أن الشمس إذا طلعت من مغربها أغلق باب التوبة، وامتنعت على من لم يكن تاب قبل ذلك، وهو معنى قوله تعالى: {يوم يأتي بعض آيات ربك لا ينفع نفساً إيمانها لم تكن آمنت من قبل} [الأنعام: 158]، الآية، هذا عند الأشاعرة، وأما عند الماتريدية: فإنما يشترط
The section on Sam'iyyat. But he renews repentance for what he committed, and regarding acceptance, their views have differed. 'But he renews repentance for what he committed' i.e., for the sin that he committed a second time. 'And regarding the way of acceptance of repentance and its modality, their views' meaning the scholars 'have differed.' The People of Truth from Ahl al-Sunnah said: It is not rationally obligatory upon God to accept the repentance of the repentant; rather nothing is obligatory upon Him, exalted, absolutely. Is its acceptance obligatory by scripture and promise? Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi said: Yes, but by a probable (dhanni) proof, since no categorical (qati') text that does not admit interpretation has been established in that regard. Our Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said: Rather, by a categorical proof. And it is known from the text that the repentance of the disbeliever is definitely accepted by scripture, due to His saying, exalted: 'Say to those who have disbelieved, if they desist, what has previously passed will be forgiven them' [al-Anfal 8:38]. The repentance of the sinful believer has two opinions: one of them, the well-known, says: its acceptance is definitive; the other, the more correct, says: its acceptance is probable. And the condition of its validity is that it occurs before the gharghara (death rattle) and before the sun rises from its west. Al-Nawawi, may God have mercy on him, said: In the state of gharghara—which is the state of agony—no repentance nor anything else is accepted. Just as when the sun rises from its west, the door of repentance is closed and it is prevented for one who had not repented before that. This is the meaning of His saying, exalted: 'The day when some signs of your Lord come, no soul will benefit from its faith if it had not believed before' [al-An'am 6:158], the verse. This is according to the Ash'aris. As for the Maturidis, rather it is conditioned...
Source pages p. 593

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the topic of repentance (tawbah) within the section on Sam'iyyat (matters known from revelation). It begins by stating the issue: a believer who commits a major sin renews repentance, but the scholars differ on whether that repentance is accepted. The author first clarifies that the Ahl al-Sunnah (Ahl al-Haqq) hold that God is not rationally obliged to accept repentance; indeed, nothing is obligatory upon Him by reason. The question then is whether acceptance is obligatory by scripture (sam'an) and by His promise. Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) and al-Qadi (Abu Bakr al-Baqillani) say yes, but the proof is probable (dhanni) because no categorical text exists. Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari says the proof is categorical (qati'). Next, the author notes that the repentance of a disbeliever (upon embracing Islam) is definitely accepted by scripture, citing the verse from Surah al-Anfal. For the sinful believer, there are two opinions: the first (well-known) says acceptance is definitive; the second (more correct) says it is probable. The passage then states the conditions for the validity of repentance: it must be performed before the gharghara (the death rattle, i.e., the throes of death) and before the sun rises from the west. A quote from Imam al-Nawawi confirms that at the moment of death agony, repentance is not accepted. Similarly, when the sun rises from the west, the door of repentance closes. This is supported by the verse from Surah al-An'am. The author concludes by noting that this ruling applies according to the Ash'aris. He begins to present the Maturidi position—'As for the Maturidis, rather it is conditioned...'—but the text breaks off at that point, leaving the Maturidi view incomplete.

إمام الحرمين
القاضي
أبو الحسن الأشعري
ظني
قطعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God is rationally obligated to accept repentance.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunnah affirm that nothing is rationally obligatory upon God; obligation is only by scripture and promise.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view of Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi regarding the scriptural obligation to accept repentance?
A) It is obligatory by a categorical (qati') proof.
B) It is not obligatory at all.
C) It is obligatory by a probable (dhanni) proof.
D) It is rationally obligatory.

Key Takeaway
God is not rationally obliged to accept repentance; the scriptural obligation is affirmed but with disagreement on whether the proof is probable or categorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you assume God will accept your repentance because it is His 'duty', or do you realize it is a gift? How does that realization change the way you repent and the urgency with which you turn back to Him?
?
This lesson shows that even scholars debated the strength of evidence for God's promise to accept repentance. Does your faith rest on definitive certainty or on hope in God's mercy despite probabilistic proofs? How does that affect your confidence in being forgiven?
?
If nothing is rationally obligatory upon God, what does that mean for your understanding of His mercy? Are you tempted to think He 'owes' you acceptance because you repented? How can you cultivate a repentance that is humble and reliant solely on His generosity?

Conditions for Valid Repentance: Before the Death Rattle and the Rising Sun
Sanusi explains the types of repentance and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for the validity of repentance, including the timing before the death rattle and before the sun rises from the west, and the distinction between the repentance of a disbeliever and a sinful believer.

Core Idea
Repentance is accepted only if done before the death rattle and before the sun rises from the west.

Having discussed the theoretical basis for repentance, the passage now turns to practical conditions. The repentance of a disbeliever upon embracing Islam is definitely accepted, while that of a sinful believer is a matter of scholarly dispute. The key condition is timing: repentance must occur before the final agony of death and before the final sign of the Hour.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of repentance
Notice that the passage distinguishes between the repentance of a disbeliever (definitely accepted) and a sinful believer (two opinions).
2
Read the condition of gharghara (death rattle)
Understand that repentance is not accepted when the soul reaches the throat. Al-Nawawi confirms this.
This is based on a well-known hadith.
3
Understand the condition of the sun rising from the west
This is a major sign of the Hour. After it, repentance is no longer accepted. The verse from Surah al-An'am supports this.
4
Note the Ash'ari position and the incomplete Maturidi view
The passage ends with 'this is according to the Ash'aris. As for the Maturidis...' but the text breaks off.
The Maturidi view may have additional conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that repentance is accepted only before the death rattle and before the sun rises from the west. Is there a sin you are planning to repent from 'later'? What if 'later' never comes? Will you repent to Allah right now, today, before you sleep?
?
The door of repentance closes with the sun rising from the west. That sign could come at any moment, yet you live as if you have endless time. How would your life change if you truly believed that you might see that sun tomorrow? What would you stop doing? What would you start doing?
?
The disbeliever's repentance is definitively accepted upon embracing Islam. You are already a Muslim, but do you live with the urgency of a new convert? Or have you become complacent, assuming years of sin can be undone in a final breath? Ask yourself: if you died in your current state, would your repentance be valid? If not, what are you waiting for?

Conditions of Repentance and the Ashʿarī-Māturīdī Disagreement
The author defines repentance, its conditions, and the disagreement between Ashʿarīs and Māturīdīs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for valid repentance and the differing views of the Ashʿarīs and Māturīdīs regarding its acceptance at the moment of death.

Core Idea
Repentance must occur before death's final signs, but scholars differ on who can benefit at that last moment.

This passage from the JaWHARA al-Tawhid commentary tackles a crucial practical question: When is repentance accepted? It presents the scriptural basis, the two opinions on certainty of acceptance, and the specific conditions regarding timing. Then it highlights an important doctrinal split between Ashʿarīs and Māturīdīs on whether the condition of avoiding the death rattle applies to all believers or only disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the scriptural foundation
Notice how the Qur'anic verse (8:38) establishes that desisting from disbelief leads to forgiveness, setting the context for repentance.
The verse is quoted to show the principle of forgiveness upon cessation of sin.
2
Identify the two opinions on acceptance
The passage presents two views on whether a believer's repentance is accepted with certainty or merely probability. Understand the distinction between 'certainly' (qaṭʿan) and 'probably' (ẓannan).
The 'more correct' view is the second, indicating scholarly preference.
3
Grasp the conditions and sectarian difference
Focus on the two conditions (before death rattle and before sunrise from west) and how al-Nawawi explains them. Then note the Ashʿarī vs. Māturīdī disagreement on who can be forgiven at the death rattle moment.
The Ashʿarīs apply the condition universally; Māturīdīs restrict it to disbelievers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تجديد التوبة بتجدد الذنب
تعالى: ﴿قُل لِلَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا إِن يَنتَهُوا يُغْفَرْ لَهُم مَّا قَدْ سَلَفَ﴾. وتوبة المؤمن العاصي فيها قولان: أحدهما المشهور يقول: بقبولها قطعاً، والآخر الأصح يقول: بقبولها ظنَّا، وشرط صحتها: صدورها قبل الغرغرة، وقبل طلوع الشمس من مغربها.
قال النووي رحمه الله تعالى: ففي حال الغرغرة ـ وهي حالة النزع - لا تُقبل توبةٌ ولا غيرها. كما أنَّ الشمس إذا طلعت من مغربها أغلق باب التوبة، وامتنعت على من لم يكن تاب قبل ذلك، وهو معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿يَوْمَ يَأْتِي بَعْضُ آيَاتِ رَبِّكَ لَا يَنفَعُ نَفْسًا إِيمَانُهَا لَمْ تَكُنْ آمَنَتْ مِن قَبْلُ﴾ الآية، هذا عند الأشاعرة، وأما عند الماتريدية: فإنما يشترط عدم الغرغرة في الكافر دون المؤمن العاصي.
Renewal of repentance due to renewal of sin. Almighty said: {Say to those who disbelieve, if they desist, what has passed will be forgiven them} [Al-Anfal 38]. And the repentance of the disobedient believer has two sayings: one, the well-known, says: it is accepted certainly; the other, the more correct, says: it is accepted probably. And its condition of validity is that it occurs before the death rattle (gargara) and before the rising of the sun from its west. Imam Nawawi, may Allah have mercy upon him, said: In the state of gargara—which is the state of death agony—no repentance or other [expiation] is accepted. Likewise, when the sun rises from its west, the door of repentance is closed and becomes prohibited for the one who had not repented before that. And that is the meaning of His saying: {On the day that some of the signs of your Lord come, no soul will benefit from its faith if it had not believed before} [Al-An'am 158] — the verse. This is according to the Ashʿarīs. But according to the Māturīdīs, the condition of absence of gargara applies only to the disbeliever, not to the disobedient believer.
Source pages p. 594

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition on repentance (tawba) and its conditions. It opens by stating the scriptural basis for forgiveness upon desisting from disbelief, quoting Quran 8:38. Then it presents two opinions on the acceptance of a believer's repentance: the well-known view holds that acceptance is certain (qaṭʿan), while the more correct view holds it is probable (ẓannan) due to the possibility that the apparent evidence is open to interpretation. The condition for valid repentance is that it occurs before the death rattle (ghargara) and before the sun rises from the west, indicating the closing of the door of repentance. The author quotes Imam al-Nawawi affirming that at the moment of death agony, no repentance or other expiation is accepted, and after the sunrise from the west the door of repentance is shut permanently, referencing Quran 6:158. Then the author notes the doctrinal difference: the Ashʿarīs hold that the condition of no gargara applies universally (both believer and disbeliever), while the Māturīdīs restrict it to the disbeliever only, meaning that a sinful believer's repentance can be accepted even at the moment of death. This passage thus outlines the conditions of repentance and a key inter-school disagreement.

Key Terms
توبة
غرغرة
قطعاً
ظنّاً
أشاعرة
ماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repentance is always accepted even at the moment of death.
Correction: According to the Ashʿarīs, repentance at the moment of death (during the death rattle) is not accepted for anyone, while the Māturīdīs hold that it is accepted for a believer but not for a disbeliever.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for repentance to be accepted?
A) It must be sincere and include all sins
B) It must occur before the death rattle and before the sun rises from the west
C) It must be accompanied by a vow
D) It must be done in public

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of repentance depends on timing and school; the Māturīdīs allow a believer's repentance even at death's final moment, unlike the Ashʿarīs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you knew that your repentance might not be accepted after the death rattle (as the Ash'aris hold), would you postpone repentance another day? What does your answer reveal about your relationship with Allah?
?
The passage presents two opinions on whether the repentance of a disobedient believer is accepted certainly or probably. Which opinion makes you more vigilant about sin, and which gives you more hope? How does that balance your fear and hope?
?
If the sun were to rise from the west tomorrow, and you had not yet repented from a specific sin, what would you wish you had done today? What is stopping you from doing that right now?

Introducing the Five Universals: Preservation of Religion
Author presents first universal and begins commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of the Five Universals (Maqāṣid al-Sharīʿa) and why preservation of religion is the first objective of Sharia.

Core Idea
The preservation of religion is the foremost universal objective of divine law.

This passage marks a shift within the section on Samʿiyyāt to discussing the higher objectives of Sharia—universal principles that all revealed laws aim to protect. The author begins by listing five essential matters in a verse line, with religion taking the first and most important position. As we read, notice how the commentary explains what 'preservation of religion' means and how it applies across different prophetic sharias.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse line and identify the five universals
Read the Arabic verse line carefully and list all five items mentioned. Note that 'reason' and 'honor' are grouped together as similar.
The verse is a single line of rajaz poetry.
2
Understand the meaning of 'preservation of religion'
The commentary explains that 'preservation of religion' means safeguarding the rulings that Allah has prescribed. Consider why this would be the first objective.
3
Distinguish between general and specific Sharia
The passage mentions two types of divine rulings: general (like the Sharia of Prophet Muhammad) and specific (like the Sharia of Prophet Jesus). Reflect on what makes a ruling general or specific.
This distinction shows that preservation applies to all revealed laws, not just one.
4
Reflect on the primacy of religion
Ask yourself why religion would take precedence over life, wealth, lineage, reason, and honor in the list. Consider the role of religion as the foundation for the other objectives.
This connects to the broader theme of Maqāṣid al-Sharīʿa.

قسم السمعيات

وَحِفْظُ دِينِ ثُمَّ نَفْسٍ مَالْ نَسَبْ وَمِثْلُهَا عَقْلٌ وَعِرْضِ قَدْ وَجَبْ

Section of Samʿiyyāt. [The Five Universals or Objectives of Sharia] And preservation of religion, then life, wealth, lineage; and similar to them are reason and honor — has become obligatory. Then he commenced upon the issue known among the people as the Five Universals, and said: 'And preservation of religion,' meaning: safeguarding it, and it is what Allah Almighty prescribed for His servants of rulings, whether general, like the Sharia of our Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), or specific, like the Sharia of Jesus (peace be upon him), then...
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage introduces a new topic within the section of Samʿiyyāt (matters derived from revelation): the Five Universals (al-Kulliyyāt al-Khams), also known as the Objectives of Sharia (Maqāṣid al-Sharīʿa). It begins with a verse line (rajaz) that enumerates the five things that must be preserved: religion (dīn), life (nafs), wealth (māl), lineage (nasab), and reason (ʿaql) and honor (ʿirḍ) — the latter two are considered either separate or combined. The passage then transitions to the commentary: the author explains the first universal 'preservation of religion' (ḥifẓ al-dīn), interpreting it as safeguarding the divinely legislated rulings. These rulings can be general, applying to all people at all times, such as the Sharia of Prophet Muhammad, or specific, applying to a particular community and time, such as the Sharia of Prophet Jesus. The commentary begins to elaborate but is cut off by the page break. The key point is that the preservation of religion is the foremost objective of the revealed law.

Misconception: The Five Universals are considered arbitrary or purely human categorizations.
Correction: The passage shows they are derived from divine legislation and are objectives that Allah Himself has prescribed, as seen in the phrase 'what Allah Almighty prescribed for His servants of rulings.'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Is there a command from Allah—like covering your hair, avoiding interest, or praying on time—that you have been treating as if it were only for a past community? What is the real reason you are not following it today?
?
Preserving religion means safeguarding the rulings of Allah. When you see fellow Muslims mocking or neglecting an obvious ruling of Allah (such as the obligation of prayer), do you speak up or stay silent? Preservation of Intellect
The author affirms the obligation to preserve intellect and cites legal rulings that protect it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that intellect, like the five universals, must be preserved, and that Islamic law prohibits its corruption through specific punishments.

Core Idea
Intellect is sacred—its preservation is obligatory, and its destruction is severely penalized.

This short passage introduces intellect as another essential universal that must be protected. Just as the Law safeguards life, property, and faith, it also prohibits anything that corrupts the mind and prescribes clear penalties for those who intentionally or accidentally harm another's intellectual capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise legal style referencing three punishments.
2
Identify the categories
The passage moves from a general obligation to preserve the intellect to specific rulings. Identify the three legal consequences mentioned for violating this obligation.
3
Reflect on the link
Consider why the Law prescribes these specific punishments. How do they protect the intellect as a universal necessity?
Think about the principle of 'prohibition of corruption' and how it is enforced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات ومثلها المذكورات في وجوب الحفظ عقل فلا يباح المفسد له ولذا شرع حد السكر والقصاص ممن أذهبه بجناية عمدا والدية في الخطأ
Section on the transmitted matters. And like them in obligation of preservation is intellect: so that which corrupts it is not permitted, and therefore the hadd punishment for intoxication is legislated, and retaliation against one who destroys it by intentional aggression, and blood money in case of error.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on sam'iyyāt (transmitted matters) but continues the discussion of universal necessities by adding intellect ('aql) and honor ('ird) as also obligatory to preserve. The author says 'like them' meaning like the previous five. Preservation of intellect means that anything that corrupts it (such as intoxicants) is not permitted. To protect intellect, the Law legislated the ḥadd punishment for drinking alcohol (sukr), and also qiṣāṣ against anyone who intentionally destroys another's intellect (e.g., by striking the head), and diya (blood money) in case of accidental destruction. This shows the importance of intellect as a fundamental human capacity.

Key Takeaway
Intellect is a fundamental human capacity that Islamic law protects by prohibiting intoxicants and prescribing retaliation or compensation for its destruction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The text says retaliation is legislated against anyone who intentionally destroys another's intellect. How can you seek forgiveness and commit to honouring others' intellect as sacred?
?
Accidental destruction of intellect still requires blood money. This shows the immense value of even unintentional harm. In your daily life, are you careless with your own intellect—by consuming information without discernment, or allowing yourself to be brainwashed by false narratives? What concrete step will you take this week to preserve the purity of your intellect?

Preserving Intellect and Honor
Commentary explains preservation of intellect and honor with legal punishments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that intellect and honor are essential aspects that must be preserved, with specific legal punishments prescribed by revelation.

Core Idea
Intellect and honor are sacred trusts protected by shariah penalties.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid explains that just as lineage is preserved, so are intellect and honor. The shariah establishes specific punishments to protect them, showing that these are not mere suggestions but binding obligations. As you read, note how each is defined and the corresponding legal measures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the commentary come through.
The passage is from the section on revealed matters (sam'iyyat).
2
Identify the two items being preserved
Find where the passage mentions 'aql (intellect) and 'ird (honor). Notice how each is introduced with 'wa mithluha' (and similar to them).
The passage compares them to lineage in obligation of preservation.
3
Understand the punishments for each
For intellect: hadd al-shurb (intoxication penalty), qisas (retaliation) for intentional harm, diya (blood-money) for error. For honor: hadd al-qadhf (penalty for slander) for the chaste, ta'zir (discretionary punishment) for others.
Note the differentiation based on intentionality and status.
4
Reflect on the revealed basis
These rulings are from the sam'iyyat, meaning they are known through revelation. Ask why revelation is necessary for such protections.
This connects to the broader theme of reliance on divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات
(وَمِثْلُهَا)، أي: المذكورات في وجوب الحفظ (عَقْلٌ) فلا يُباح المُفْسِدُ له ; ولذا شُرعَ حَدُّ السُّكر والقصاص ممَّن أذهَبَهُ بجناية عمدا، والدِّية في الخطأ ، ( وَعِرْضُ ) (۲) كذلك ، وهو : موضع المدح والذم (۳) من الإنسان ; فلا يُباح بقذف (٤) ولا بسب ; ولذا شُرعَ حدُّ القَذف للعفيف (۱) ، والتّعزير لغيره (۲ ) .
Section on revealed matters. And similar to them, meaning the aforementioned in obligation of preservation, is intellect; so what corrupts it is not permitted; hence the penalty for intoxication and retaliation from whoever destroys it by intentional injury, and blood-money for error, are prescribed. And honor similarly, and it is the locus of praise and blame in a person; so it may not be violated by slander nor by insult; hence the penalty of slander for the chaste is prescribed, and discretionary punishment for other than it.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on the preservation of intellect and honor as part of the revealed matters (al-sam'iyyat). It begins by stating that just as lineage is to be preserved, so too intellect and honor must be safeguarded from corruption. For intellect, any act that destroys or impairs it is prohibited, and the shariah has prescribed specific punishments: the penalty for intoxication (hadd al-shurb), retaliation (qisas) for intentionally causing loss of intellect, and blood-money (diya) for unintentional harm. For honor, defined as the locus of praise and blame in a person, it may not be violated by slander (qadhf) or insult (sabb). The shariah prescribes the penalty of slander (hadd al-qadhf) for a chaste person, and discretionary punishment (ta'zir) for others. The passage follows the order of the kulliyat by analogy.

التعزير

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think honor is equated with lineage in all respects, but the passage only states the obligation of preservation, not identical rulings.
Correction: The passage says 'wa mithluha' meaning similar in the obligation to preserve, but the specific punishments differ.

Checkpoint

A) Hadd al-shurb
B) Qisas
C) Diya

Key Takeaway

The Hierarchy of the Five Universal Necessities
Enumeration and ranking of the five universal necessities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the order of the five necessities in Islamic law and the rationale for placing religion first.

Core Idea
The preservation of religion is the goal; all other necessities serve it.

In this lesson, we examine the classification of the five universal necessities that Islamic law aims to preserve. The author presents a clear hierarchy starting with religion, then life, intellect, lineage, and wealth, with honor placed accordingly. Understanding this order is foundational to Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the author's own sequence of the five universals.
Notice the phrase 'وأكد الخمسة الدين' which establishes religion as the strongest.
2
Identify the five categories and their order
List the five universals in the order given: religion, lives, intellects, lineages, wealth. Note where honor (al-a'rad) is placed.
Honor can be in the rank of wealth unless it involves cutting lineage.
3
Reflect on the rationale for the order
Consider why religion is first: because preserving the others is a means to preserve religion. Think about the wisdom in this hierarchy.
This ordering is not arbitrary; it reflects the purposes of the shariah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترتيب الكليات الخمس
[ تَرتيبُ الكُلَّيَّات الخَمْس ]
وأكد الخمسة (۳) الدِّينُ؛ لأنَّ حفظَ غيره وسيلةٌ لحفظهِ، ثُمَّ حفظ النُّفوس ، ثُمَّ العُقول ، ثُمَّ الأَنْسَاب ، ثُمَّ الأموال، وفي مرتبتها الأعراض إن لم تؤد الإذاية فيها إلى قطع نَسب ، وإلا (٤) كانت في مرتبة الأنساب (قَدْ وَجَب) حفظ الجميع في جميع الشرائع (٥) ، لشرفها
فإن قلت: يَرِدُ عليه أن شرب الخمر كان جائزا أول الإسلام ،بوحي، وتكرر نسخه ثلاث مرات، أجيب: بأن المراد حفظ الخمسة ، أو باعتبار ما استقر عليه أمر ملتنا الشنواني. وقال في «هداية المريد»: ما جزم به الناظم من أنَّ حفظ العقول لم يزل مُحترما ، هو طريق الأصوليين كما صرح به القرطبي والأبي في شرحيهما لصحيح مسلم، ووجهه القرطبي: بأن الشرائع مصالح للعباد، وأصل المصالح العقلُ ؛ فيحرم كل ما يذهبه أو يشوشه ، ويُجاب عن حديث شرب حمزة=
Ordering of the five universals. [Ordering of the five universals] And the strongest of the five is Religion, because the preservation of others is a means to its preservation, then preservation of lives, then intellects, then lineages, then wealth, and in their rank are honors if the harm in them does not lead to severance of lineage, otherwise they are in the rank of lineages. Indeed, the preservation of all has been obligated in all revealed laws, due to their nobility. For if you say: There is an objection that drinking wine was initially permissible in early Islam by revelation, and its abrogation occurred three times, the answer is: What is meant is the preservation of the five, or by consideration of what the matter of our religion settled upon. Al-Shanwani. And he said in Hidayat al-Murid: That to which the author asserted that the preservation of intellect has never ceased to be respected is the way of the usuliyyun as stated by al-Qurtubi and al-Ubbi in their commentaries on Sahih Muslim, and al-Qurtubi's explanation is that the laws are benefits for the servants, and the foundation of benefits is the intellect; so everything that eliminates or confuses it is prohibited, and it is answered about the hadith of Hamza's drinking...
Source pages p. 598

Commentary
This passage classifies and orders the five universal necessities (al-kulliyat al-khams) of the shariah: religion, life, intellect, lineage, and wealth. It begins by stating that the most important is religion, because the preservation of the others serves the preservation of religion. Then follow life, intellect, lineage, and wealth. Honor (al-a'rad) is placed in the same rank as wealth, unless the harms involve severance of lineage, in which case it moves to the lineage rank. The author asserts that the preservation of all these is obligatory in all revealed laws. An objection is raised: in early Islam, wine (which destroys intellect) was permissible by revelation, and its prohibition was abrogated three times, suggesting that intellect was not always preserved. The answer given is that the preservation of the five is meant either in the settled state of the shariah (after abrogation) or that the reference is to the final form of our religion. Al-Shanwani adds from Hidayat al-Murid that the early permissibility of wine was a temporary exception, and the principle is that preserving intellect is always required as it is the foundation of all benefits, and thus the hadith of Hamza's drinking (which may have been before the complete prohibition) does not undermine the principle.

Key Terms
الكليات الخمس
الدين
النفوس
العقول
الأنساب
الأموال
الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is religion placed first in the ordering?
A) Because it was revealed first
B) Because preserving others is a means to preserve religion
C) Because it is the most important without further reason
D) Because it includes all other necessities

Key Takeaway
The five necessities are ranked with religion as the highest, and all laws aim to preserve them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about your daily choices: when faced with a conflict between protecting your religion and protecting your life, which have you actually prioritized? Recall a specific moment and be honest—did your action reflect the hierarchy taught here? What will you change starting today?
?
You value your intellect—your ability to think, learn, and reason. But do you use that intellect primarily to pursue worldly gains or to deepen your understanding of your religion? If your intellect is a trust from Allah, are you investing it in what He loves most?
?
Consider your attachment to your lineage (family honor) and your wealth. Have you ever compromised a religious obligation to protect your family's reputation or to avoid financial loss? After learning that religion is the highest necessity, what is one commitment you can make to put religion above these?

Resolving the Objection on Early Permissibility of Wine
Objection and answer regarding the permissibility of wine in early Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars answer the objection that wine was initially permissible despite being against preserving intellect.

Core Idea
The final settled shariah, not early exceptions, defines the rule.

This lesson addresses a common objection: if preserving intellect is essential, why was wine allowed in early Islam? The answer distinguishes between the final form of the religion and temporary allowances, emphasizing that the ultimate goal is preservation of the five.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the objection about wine and the provided answer.
The objection points out that wine was initially permissible by revelation and abrogated three times.
2
Analyze the two parts of the answer
The answer first says 'المراد حفظ الخمسة' and then mentions 'باعتبار ما استقر عليه أمر ملتنا'. Understand that the reference is to the final settled state.
Al-Shanwani adds that the preservation is considered in the final form of the religion.
3
Learn al-Qurtubi's principle
Read al-Qurtubi's explanation: laws are benefits, and the foundation of benefits is intellect, so anything harming it is prohibited.
This reinforces that the early allowance was a temporary exception, not a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترتيب الكليات الخمس
[ تَرتيبُ الكُلَّيَّات الخَمْس ]
وأكد الخمسة (۳) الدِّينُ؛ لأنَّ حفظَ غيره وسيلةٌ لحفظهِ، ثُمَّ حفظ النُّفوس ، ثُمَّ العُقول ، ثُمَّ الأَنْسَاب ، ثُمَّ الأموال، وفي مرتبتها الأعراض إن لم تؤد الإذاية فيها إلى قطع نَسب ، وإلا (٤) كانت في مرتبة الأنساب (قَدْ وَجَب) حفظ الجميع في جميع الشرائع (٥) ، لشرفها
فإن قلت: يَرِدُ عليه أن شرب الخمر كان جائزا أول الإسلام ،بوحي، وتكرر نسخه ثلاث مرات، أجيب: بأن المراد حفظ الخمسة ، أو باعتبار ما استقر عليه أمر ملتنا الشنواني. وقال في «هداية المريد»: ما جزم به الناظم من أنَّ حفظ العقول لم يزل مُحترما ، هو طريق الأصوليين كما صرح به القرطبي والأبي في شرحيهما لصحيح مسلم، ووجهه القرطبي: بأن الشرائع مصالح للعباد، وأصل المصالح العقلُ ؛ فيحرم كل ما يذهبه أو يشوشه ، ويُجاب عن حديث شرب حمزة=
Ordering of the five universals. [Ordering of the five universals] And the strongest of the five is Religion, because the preservation of others is a means to its preservation, then preservation of lives, then intellects, then lineages, then wealth, and in their rank are honors if the harm in them does not lead to severance of lineage, otherwise they are in the rank of lineages. Indeed, the preservation of all has been obligated in all revealed laws, due to their nobility. For if you say: There is an objection that drinking wine was initially permissible in early Islam by revelation, and its abrogation occurred three times, the answer is: What is meant is the preservation of the five, or by consideration of what the matter of our religion settled upon. Al-Shanwani. And he said in Hidayat al-Murid: That to which the author asserted that the preservation of intellect has never ceased to be respected is the way of the usuliyyun as stated by al-Qurtubi and al-Ubbi in their commentaries on Sahih Muslim, and al-Qurtubi's explanation is that the laws are benefits for the servants, and the foundation of benefits is the intellect; so everything that eliminates or confuses it is prohibited, and it is answered about the hadith of Hamza's drinking...
Source pages p. 598

Commentary
This passage classifies and orders the five universal necessities (al-kulliyat al-khams) of the shariah: religion, life, intellect, lineage, and wealth. It begins by stating that the most important is religion, because the preservation of the others serves the preservation of religion. Then follow life, intellect, lineage, and wealth. Honor (al-a'rad) is placed in the same rank as wealth, unless the harms involve severance of lineage, in which case it moves to the lineage rank. The author asserts that the preservation of all these is obligatory in all revealed laws. An objection is raised: in early Islam, wine (which destroys intellect) was permissible by revelation, and its prohibition was abrogated three times, suggesting that intellect was not always preserved. The answer given is that the preservation of the five is meant either in the settled state of the shariah (after abrogation) or that the reference is to the final form of our religion. Al-Shanwani adds from Hidayat al-Murid that the early permissibility of wine was a temporary exception, and the principle is that preserving intellect is always required as it is the foundation of all benefits, and thus the hadith of Hamza's drinking (which may have been before the complete prohibition) does not undermine the principle.

Key Terms
شرب الخمر
النسخ
الأصوليون
القرطبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The early permissibility of wine proves that preserving intellect is not always required in Islamic law.
Correction: The principle of preserving intellect is constant; early allowances were temporary exceptions that were later abrogated, and the final ruling prohibits anything harming intellect.

Checkpoint
How does al-Qurtubi explain the prohibition of things that harm the intellect?
A) It is a revealed command without further reason
B) Because intellect is the foundation of benefits in the laws
C) Because it was abrogated three times
D) Because the early Muslims agreed on it

Key Takeaway
Preservation of intellect is a constant principle; temporary allowances do not negate it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a past exception or a weaker opinion to justify a current sin? How does the principle of the settled final ruling challenge your rationalization?
?
If all laws are meant to benefit you and protect your intellect, how are you currently harming your intellect through neglect or indulgence? What step will you take today to honor that trust?
?
The early permission for wine was a temporary concession, but you are now commanded to obey the final prohibition. What temporary concessions in your prayer, fasting, or dhikr are you still clinging to instead of embracing the full command?

Hadith Evidence for Preserving Religion and Intellect
Al-Laqqani cites a hadith as evidence for preserving religion and deduces preservation of intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Laqqani uses a hadith to establish the preservation of religion and intellect as necessary implications of Islamic law.

Core Idea
From protecting blood, wealth, and honor comes protecting religion and intellect.

Al-Laqqani begins the section on transmitted matters (sam'iyyat) by grounding the preservation of religion and intellect in a well-known hadith. This passage shows how a single prophetic statement can carry multiple levels of legal and theological implication. By reflecting on the hadith's prohibitions, the author deduces obligations that go beyond the explicit text.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its immediate ruling
Notice how the hadith declares blood, wealth, and honor sacred. This is the basis for the preservation of religion (hifz al-adyan).
2
Trace the logical chain
Observe the shift from preservation of religion to preservation of lineage (under honor) to preservation of intellect. This is a deductive move: if religion must be preserved, then the intellect (needed to understand religion) must also be preserved.
3
Understand the concept of sam'iyyat
This passage introduces the category of transmitted matters. Recognise that the hadith is a sam'i proof, meaning it is based on revelation, not reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات كما أخبر بذلك شرعنا، كقوله: (فإن دماءكم وأموالكم وأعراضكم عليكم حرام الحديث، وفي آخره: «ألا لا ترجعوا بعدي كفارًا، يضرب بعضكم رقاب بعض») وهذا لحفظ الأديان، كما أن حفظ الأنساب داخل تحت حفظ الأعراض، ومن لازم التكليف بذلك التكليف بحفظ العقل، والله تعالى أعلم.
Section on the transmitted matters: As our Law has informed of that, such as His statement: 'Indeed your blood, your wealth, and your honor are sacred among you' — hadith, and at its end: 'Beware, do not turn back after me as disbelievers, striking one another's necks.' And this is for the preservation of religions. Just as the preservation of lineages is included under the preservation of honors, and from the requirement of that obligation comes the obligation to preserve the intellect. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 599

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the sam'iyyat (transmitted matters). It presents a hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that emphasizes the sanctity of life, property, and honor, and warns against returning to disbelief and killing each other. The author states that this hadith is evidence for the preservation of religions (hifz al-adyan). He then links preservation of lineage to preservation of honor. Finally, he argues that the obligation to preserve these entails the obligation to preserve the intellect, because intellect is necessary for understanding and maintaining religion. This is a logical deduction from the overall aim of the Law.

Key Terms
السمعيات
حفظ الأديان
حفظ الأنساب
حفظ الأعراض
حفظ العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Preserving intellect is not explicitly commanded in the hadith, so it is not an obligation.
Correction: Al-Laqqani shows it is a necessary implication from the obligation to preserve religion, thus it is a rational deduction from the same source.

Checkpoint
How does preservation of intellect follow from the hadith according to the passage?
A) It is explicitly mentioned in the hadith.
B) It is a necessary implication from the obligation to preserve religion.
C) It is deduced from the prohibition of killing.
D) It is unrelated to the hadith.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to preserve intellect is a necessary implication of the duty to preserve religion, derived from the hadith on the sanctity of life, wealth, and honor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that protecting life, wealth, and honor leads to preserving religion. What is the state of your own commitment to protecting these in your community? Have you neglected any of these obligations, and how will you rectify that starting today?
?
The author deduces that preserving intellect is a necessary requirement of preserving religion. Are you actively nurturing your intellect for the sake of your religion? What specific step will you take this week to increase your knowledge or understanding?
?
Consider the Prophet's warning against turning back as disbelievers. In what subtle ways might you be drifting from your faith? What will you do to reaffirm your loyalty to Allah and His Messenger?
