The Ruling on Denying a Matter of Consensus
Al-Bajuri presents the ruling on denying decisive consensus and defines it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which denying a consensus ruling constitutes unbelief, and the definition of decisive consensus.

Core Idea
Not every denial of consensus is unbelief; only decisive consensus known by necessity.

Al-Bajuri addresses a critical issue in Islamic theology: when does rejecting a consensus ruling cross into unbelief? He presents two views and clarifies that only denial of a decisive consensus known from religion by necessity merits such a grave judgment. This passage establishes the criteria for identifying which matters are beyond dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic text and its English translation. Notice the flow from presenting the first opinion to giving the correct one and then defining key terms.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'إجماع قطعي' and 'معلوم من الدين بالضرورة' that are central to the discussion.
2
Identify the two opinions
Find the first opinion (the author's view) and the second opinion (al-Bajuri's preferred view). Note how al-Bajuri labels the first as weak and the second as the truth.
The first opinion says the denier becomes an unbeliever and is killed; the second says only if the consensus is decisive and known by necessity.
3
Examine the definition of decisive consensus
Read the definition carefully. It has two conditions: (1) agreement of the reliable authorities, (2) each explicitly states the ruling, with no dissenter. The reason given is that it is impossible by custom for them all to err.
The term 'المعتبَرون' refers to qualified scholars; 'إحالة العادة' means impossibility by custom.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters. Not all consensus rulings are equal; only those that are decisively established and known by necessity can be the basis for takfir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَنْ نَفَى المُجْمَعِ أي : كُلُّ مُكلَّف جحد حكما مجمعا عليه إجماعاً قطعيًّا، أي فيكفر بجحده ويُقتل ، وهذا ضعيف وإن جزمَ النَّاظم به ، والحقُّ القول الثاني: أنَّه لا يكفر نافي حكم الإجماع إلَّا إذا كان قطعيًّا معلوما من الدين بالضرورة.
والإجماعُ القَطعي : هو ما أَتَّفق المعتبَرونَ على كونه إجماعاً، بأن صَرَّحَ كُلٌّ مِن المُجْمِعِينَ بالحُكمِ الَّذِي أجمعوا عليه مِن غير أن يَشُذَّ منهم أحدٌ ؛ لإحالة العادة خطأَهُم.
Whoever denies the consensus (al-mujma‘) – i.e., every legally responsible person who rejects a ruling that is a matter of consensus by a decisive consensus – then he becomes an unbeliever by his denial and is to be killed. But this is weak, even though the author (al-nāẓim) asserts it. The truth is the second opinion: that the denier of a ruling of consensus does not become an unbeliever unless it is decisive and known from religion by necessity (ma‘lūm min al-dīn bi-ḍarūrah). And the decisive consensus (al-ijmā‘ al-qaṭ‘ī) is what the reliable authorities agree constitutes a consensus, such that each of those who form the consensus explicitly states the ruling on which they have reached consensus, with none of them dissenting, because it is impossible by custom (li-iḥālat al-‘ādah) that they err.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (ḥukm) on the issue of denying a matter of consensus. Al-Bajuri begins by presenting the first opinion: that whoever rejects a ruling that is established by decisive consensus becomes an unbeliever and is to be killed. This is the view of the author of the poem (al-nāẓim, i.e., al-Laqqānī). However, al-Bajuri immediately qualifies it as weak (ḍa‘īf). The correct view (al-ḥaqq) is the second: that denial of a consensus ruling is only unbelief if the consensus is decisive (qaṭ‘ī) and the matter is known from religion by necessity (ma‘lūm min al-dīn bi-ḍarūrah). He then defines decisive consensus: it is the agreement of all qualified scholars (al-mu‘tabarūn) on a ruling, with each explicitly stating it, and no dissenter, because it is impossible by custom that they all err simultaneously. This definition establishes the standard for a consensus that can be the basis for a charge of unbelief.

Key Terms
نفي المجمع
إجماع قطعي
معلوم من الدين بالضرورة
المعتبَرون
إحالة العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that denying any consensus is unbelief.
Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that only denial of a decisive consensus known from religion by necessity constitutes unbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in this passage, what two conditions must be met for the denial of a consensus to constitute unbelief?
A) The consensus must be unanimous and the matter a major sin.
B) The consensus must be decisive and the matter known from religion by necessity.
C) The consensus must be explicit and the denier must be a scholar.
D) The consensus must be about a matter of worship and the denier persistent.

Key Takeaway
The denial of a consensus only leads to unbelief if the consensus is decisive and the matter is known from religion by necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been too quick to judge someone's faith based on a matter you assumed was consensus but was not decisive? How does this passage reshape your responsibility in such judgments?
?
Knowing that even scholars disagree on this ruling, what does that teach you about humility when it comes to categorizing others as Muslim or non-Muslim?
?
The passage warns that only matters known by necessity from religion can be grounds for takfir. Is your own conviction about the essentials of Islam built on such certain foundations, or on convenience? What would change if you held yourself to that standard?

When Does Deeming a Sin Permissible Become Unbelief?
Al-Bajuri presents the Ash'ari and Maturidi views on deeming sins permissible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly positions on when deeming a prohibited matter permissible (istiḥlāl) results in unbelief, and the key distinctions between the Ash'ari and Maturidi views.

Core Idea
Deeming a sin permissible is not automatically unbelief; it depends on the type of prohibition and the school.

Al-Bajuri now addresses a pivotal question in theology of faith: when does treating a prohibited act as permissible cross the line into unbelief? The Ash'aris and Maturidis differ on the conditions. This passage presents these views clearly, focusing on the concept of istiḥlāl and the role of consensus and decisive proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Get an overview of the different positions presented.
Notice that the passage starts by defining 'deeming permissible' as believing a consensus-prohibited matter to be licit.
2
Identify the three views
The passage presents three views: the Ash'ari view, and two Maturidi views. Locate each and note their conditions.
The Ash'ari view requires actual belief in permissibility; the Maturidi views involve decisive proof and type of prohibition.
3
Understand the key distinction
The second Maturidi view distinguishes between prohibition for the act's essence (li-'aynihi) and prohibition for an external reason (like fasting on Eid). Understand why this matters.
If prohibition is for the essence and proven decisively, deeming it permissible is unbelief; otherwise not.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how these views impact the determination of faith and unbelief in practice. The passage shows that unbelief is not automatic.
This connects to the broader theme of 'signs of denial' (amarat al-takdhib).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حُكم مُستَحلَّ المَعاصِي
ثُمَّ عَطفَ على قوله : من نفى لمجمع قوله: (أَوْ اسْتَبَاحَ) ، أي : اعتقد إباحةَ مُحرَّم مجمع عليه ولو صغيرة معلوم من الدين تحريمه بالضرورة (كَالزِّنَا) واللواط ولو في مملوكه ؛ فلا يكفر بفعل شيء من ذلك إلَّا معَ الاستحلال، هذا مذهب الأشاعرة.
وقال بعض الماتريديَّة: استحلال المعصية ولو صغيرةٌ كُفْرٌ ، إذا ثبت كونها معصية بدليل قطعي ؛ لأنَّ ذلكَ مِن أمارات التكذيب.
وقال البعض الآخر : مَن اعتقدَ حِلَّ مُحرَّم؛ فإنْ كانَ تحريمه لعينه كالزنا وشرب الخمر ـ وقد ثبت بدليل قطعي - كفر ، وإلا فلا، كما إذا استحلَّ صومَ يوم العيد
وبين هذا المعطوفِ وما عُطف عليه تلازم أو تساو؛ فما ذكره المُصنِّفُ صريحاً إلَّا تبعاً للقوم ، وإرادة التنصيص على أعيان المسائل ، وزيادة الإيضاح وقوله: (فَلْتَسْمَعِ) تَكمِلةٌ.
The Ruling on the One Who Deems Sins Permissible
Then He attached to His saying 'whoever denies consensus' His saying 'or deems permissible' – i.e., believes the permissibility of a prohibited matter that is a matter of consensus, even if it is a minor sin whose prohibition is known from religion by necessity, such as fornication and sodomy, even with his slave; for he does not become an unbeliever by doing any of that unless with deeming it permissible; this is the school of the Ash‘arīs. And some of the Māturīdiyyah said: deeming permissible a sin, even a minor one, is unbelief, if it is established that it is a sin by a decisive proof, because that is among the signs of denial (amārāt al-takdhīb). And others said: whoever believes the licitness of a prohibited thing – if its prohibition is for its very essence, like fornication and drinking wine – and it is established by a decisive proof, he becomes an unbeliever; otherwise not, as when one deems permissible fasting on the day of Eid. And between this conjoined clause and what it is conjoined to there is necessary concomitance or equivalence; so what the author mentioned explicitly is only following the scholars, and intending to specify particular issues, and for additional clarification. And his saying 'so let it be heard' is a completion.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of different positions on deeming prohibited things permissible (istiḥlāl) under the section of sam‘iyyāt. Al-Bajuri begins by quoting the line from Jawharat al-Tawḥīd: 'or deems permissible,' and explains it as believing a prohibited matter that is subject to consensus to be licit, even a minor sin like fornication, provided its prohibition is known by necessity. The Ash‘arī position is that unbelief only occurs when one actually deems it permissible, not merely by committing the sin. He then cites two Māturīdī views: first, some Māturīdīs say that deeming any sin permissible, even a minor one, is unbelief if the sin’s prohibition is established by a decisive proof, because it indicates denial (takdhīb). Second, other Māturīdīs make a distinction: if the prohibition is for the act’s essence (li-‘aynihi) and is proven by a decisive proof, then deeming it permissible is unbelief; otherwise not, as with fasting on Eid, whose prohibition is for an external reason. Al-Bajuri then remarks on the relationship between this clause and the previous one about denying consensus: some say they are necessarily concomitant or equivalent, but he notes that the author only mentioned them separately for clarity and to specify issues. He concludes with a note on the phrase 'so let it be heard' as a completion of the discussion.

Key Terms
استحلال
الاستباحة
محرم
تحريم لعينه
أمارات التكذيب
الأشاعرة
الماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that committing a sin is automatically unbelief if one deems it permissible, regardless of the school.
Correction: The Ash'ari view requires the actual belief in permissibility, not just the act. Even deeming it permissible may not be unbelief in some Maturidi views if the prohibition is not for the essence or not decisively proven.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari school as presented in this passage, when does a person become an unbeliever regarding a prohibited act that is a matter of consensus?
A) By committing the act even without deeming it permissible.
B) Only by deeming the act permissible (istiḥlāl).
C) By denying that it is prohibited.
D) By committing the act publicly.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school holds that unbelief from deeming a sin permissible only occurs with the actual belief in its permissibility, while Māturīdīs differ based on the type of proof and whether the prohibition is for the act's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that a 'small' sin is not really that bad, or that the prohibition is not absolute? According to the Maturidi view, could that mindset be an 'indicator of denial' (amārāt al-takdhīb)? Examine your heart and ask: Do I truly submit to Allah's prohibition in every matter, even in minor sins? If not, what is holding you back from full surrender?
?
The Ash'ari school distinguishes between committing a sin and actually deeming it permissible. Are you aware of any area in your life where you have unconsciously justified a sin by reinterpreting the Sharia? Fasting on Eid, for example, is prohibited for an external reason—but what about acts where the prohibition is clear and decisive? Are you willing to uproot any secret belief that a clear prohibition is negotiable?
?
Imagine you are confronted with a situation where committing a sin seems convenient or even beneficial. Your mind may start rationalizing. The second Maturidi view warns that deeming permissible a sin whose prohibition is for its essence (like zina or alcohol) is unbelief. Do you feel a tremor in your heart at that thought? How would you protect your faith from the subtle inclination to make halal what Allah has made haram? Commit to one concrete action today to guard against istihlal.

Appointing an Imam: Obligation by Shari'ah
Statement of communal obligation to appoint an imam by Shari'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the appointment of an imam is a communal obligation based on Shari'ah, not reason, and what imamate means in classical Sunni thought.

Core Idea
Appointing an imam is a duty from God, not from human logic.

This lesson examines the foundational claim that the obligation to appoint an imam comes from divine revelation, not rational necessity. The author states this clearly in the poem and then explains the nature of the obligation as communal (fard kifaya). We will explore the definition of imamate as the supreme caliphate and the role of the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) in fulfilling this duty.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and opening statement
Begin by reading the line of poetry: 'And the appointment of a just imam is obligatory by the Shari'ah, so know, not by reason.' This sets the central thesis.
Notice the emphasis on 'by Shari'ah' and 'not by reason' – this distinguishes Islamic theology from rationalist views.
2
Understand the obligation type
The commentary explains that the obligation is communal (fard kifaya). Reflect on what this means: the entire community is responsible, but if some undertake it, the rest are absolved.
3
Identify who appoints the imam
The passage specifies that the duty is fulfilled by 'the people of loosening and binding' (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd). Learn who these individuals are and why their role is crucial.
These are scholars, leaders, and notables whose consensus carries weight.
4
Define imamate as caliphate
The author defines imamate as 'general leadership in religious and worldly affairs as a deputyship from the Prophet.' Contemplate the scope of authority and its basis in revelation.
5
Examine the conditions and functions
Review the footnotes that elaborate on when the obligation applies (when no imam is designated) and the functions of an imam (implementing law, defending borders, etc.).
Even if the imam is not perfectly religious, still obligatory to appoint him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ود
قسم السمعيات
مبحثُ الإِمَامَة أو الخِلَافة العُظمى
١٣٠. وَوَاجِبُ نَصْبُ إِمَامٍ عَدْلِ بالشَّرْعِ فَاعْلَمْ لَا بِحُكْمِ العَقْلِ
ثُمَّ شرعَ في مباحث الإمامة تبعاً للقوم (۱) وإن كانتْ مِن الفقهيات ، فقالَ: (وَوَاجِبٌ) على الأُمَّة (٢) وجوبًا كِفائيًّا (نَصْبُ إِمَامٍ)، أي: إقامته وتَوْلِيتُه(۳) ؛ فيُخاطَبُ بذلك جميع الأمة ، من ابتداء موتها إلى قيام الساعة ، فإذا قام به أهل الحل والعقد(٤) سقط عن غيرهم، لا فرق في ذلك بين زمن الفتنة ،وغيره ، هذا مذهب أهل السُّنَّة وأكثر المعتزلة ومتى أطلقت الإمامة انصرفت ، للخلافة، وهي: رئاسةٌ عامة في أمور الدين والدُّنيا نيابةً عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم
(۱) قوله: (تبعا للقوم) لكثرة اختلاف الفرق الضالة فيها كما يأتي . الأمير . (۲) قوله : ( على الأمة أي عند عدم النصب من الله أو رسوله على التولية لمعين، وعند عدم استخلاف السابق غيره، وإلا فلا يجب النصب على الأمة ، بل يكون المعين أو المستخلف إماما ، ولو خلي الزمان عن سلطان ذي كفاية فالأمور مَوكُولة للعلماء ، ويلزم الأمة الرجوع إليهم، ويصيرون ولاة، فإن عسر جمعهم على واحد اشتغل كل قطر باتباع علمائه، فإن كثروا فالمتبع أعلمهم، فإن استووا قرع، وهذا من حيث انعقاد الولاية العامة، فلا ينافي وجوب متابعة العلماء مطلقا . الشنواني . (۳) قوله : ( نصب إمام ليقوم بتنفيذ أحكامهم وإقامة حدودهم ، وسد ثغورهم وتجهيز جيوشهم، وقهر المُتغلبة والمُتَلصِّصَة وقطاع الطريق ، وإقامة الجمع والأعياد، وقطع المنازعات الواقعة بين العباد، والواجب نصبُ إمام وإن لم يسر على طريقة المصطفى من الزهد في الدنيا. الشنواني . (٤) قوله: (أهل الحَلَّ والعَقد) أي: حل الأمور وعقدها ، وهم العلماء والرؤساء ووجوه الناس الذين يتيسر اجتماعهم عند البيعة ، أما بيعة غير أهل الحل والعقد فلا عبرة بها . الشنواني.
Waw Dal. Section of Sam'iyyat. Topic of Imamate or the Supreme Caliphate. 130. And the appointment of a just imam is obligatory by the Shari'ah, so know, not by reason. Then he began the topics of imamate following the people (1) even though they are from the fiqhiyyat, so he said: (And it is obligatory) upon the ummah (2) as a communal obligation (the appointment of an imam), i.e. establishing and installing him (3); so all the ummah are addressed by that from the beginning of its death until the establishment of the Hour; when the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) undertake it (4), it falls from others; there is no difference in that between a time of fitnah and otherwise; this is the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunnah and most of the Mu'tazila. And when imamate is mentioned absolutely, it refers to the caliphate; and it is: a general leadership in the affairs of religion and this world as a deputyship from the Prophet (peace be upon him). (1) His saying: "following the people" because of the great disagreement of the deviant sects in it as will come. – al-Amir. (2) His saying: "upon the ummah" meaning in the absence of appointment by Allah or His messenger of a specific person, and in the absence of a previous caliph appointing a successor; otherwise the obligation of appointment does not fall on the ummah, but the designated or appointed becomes imam. If the time is left without a ruler of sufficient skill, then matters are entrusted to the scholars, and the ummah must refer to them, and they become rulers. If it is difficult to gather them upon one, each region follows its scholars; if there are many, then the most learned is followed; if they are equal, then they cast lots. This is regarding the establishment of general authority, and it does not contradict the absolute obligation of following the scholars. – al-Shinwani. (3) His saying: "appointment of an imam" to implement their legal judgments, establish punishments, secure borders, equip armies, subdue aggressors, thieves, and highway robbers, establish Friday and Eid prayers, and settle disputes between people. It is obligatory to appoint an imam even if he does not follow the path of the Chosen One (PBUH) in asceticism from this world. – al-Shinwani. (4) His saying: "people of loosening and binding" meaning those who solve and bind matters; they are the scholars, leaders, and notables of the people whose gathering is feasible at the time of allegiance; as for the allegiance of other than ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd, it is not considered. – al-Shinwani.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement on the obligation of appointing an imam. It begins with the line of the poem: 'And the appointment of a just imam is obligatory by the Shari'ah, not by reason.' The author then explains that the discussion of imamate follows the tradition of earlier scholars, even though it is a fiqh (jurisprudential) matter. He asserts that the obligation is communal (fard kifaya), addressed to the entire community, from the time the community begins until the end of time. The duty is fulfilled when the 'people of loosening and binding' (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) appoint an imam, after which it falls from others. This applies in times of peace and fitnah. The author notes that this is the position of Ahl al-Sunnah and most Mu'tazila. He then defines imamate as the caliphate, describing it as general authority over religious and worldly affairs on behalf of the Prophet (peace be upon him). The footnotes expand on the conditions for the obligation: it is only required when no specific imam has been designated by God, the Prophet, or a previous caliph. If there is no suitable ruler, the scholars assume authority. The footnote also lists the functions of the imam, including implementing laws, defending borders, and settling disputes, and states that the imam must be appointed even if he is not an ascetic. Finally, the definition of ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd is provided.

Key Terms
إمامة
خلافة
وجوب كفائي
أهل الحل والعقد
سمعيات
شرع
عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the obligation to appoint an imam is based on reason alone.
Correction: The poem and commentary explicitly state it is by Shari'ah, not reason, grounding the duty in divine command.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what type of obligation is the appointment of an imam?
A) Individual obligation (fard 'ayn)
B) Communal obligation (fard kifaya)
C) Recommended (mandub)
D) Permissible (mubah)

Key Takeaway
Appointing an imam is a communal religious duty based on divine law, not rational inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that appointing a just imam is a collective duty from Allah, not from your own logic. How does this challenge the way you think about political leadership in your own context? Are you actively seeking or supporting leadership that is established according to Shari'ah, or have you reduced this obligation to a mere political preference? What is one concrete step you can take this week to fulfill your share of this communal duty?
?
The passage says the obligation remains even in times of fitnah. When you see chaos and division in the Muslim world, do you resign yourself to despair, or do you recognize your responsibility to contribute to the appointment of a just leader? What is holding you back from being part of the solution?
?
The imam is defined as a deputy of the Prophet. How does this high view of leadership affect your respect for those in authority? Do you view your leaders as mere politicians, or as trustees of the Prophet's mission? What does that demand from you in terms of obedience and support?

The Condition of Justice (ʿAdl) for the Imam
Defines justice (ʿadl) and its components for the imam, and lists those excluded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of justice (ʿadl) as a condition for the imamate and which individuals are excluded from this qualification.

Core Idea
Justice for the imam is a legal composite of five specific attributes.

The imamate, as supreme political leadership, requires certain qualities in the person holding it. The first and most detailed condition Sanusi discusses is justice (ʿadl). But this is not a general moral term; it is a precise legal concept composed of five elements that together ensure the imam is capable and trustworthy. In this lesson, we unpack that definition and see who is excluded by it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on justice
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the definition of ʿadl and the composite description.
Notice that Sanusi provides two linguistic interpretations before settling on the technical meaning.
2
Identify the five components
List the five conditions: Islam, puberty, reason, freedom, and absence of sin.
3
Understand the exclusions
Note why children, insane, slaves, women, hermaphrodites, sinners, and oppressors are excluded.
The reasoning is functional and social, not merely religious.
4
Reflect on the reasoning
Why are these exclusions based on capability and social authority?
5
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to test your grasp.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من خمسة شروط:
ووصف الإمام بقوله: (عدل) وهو الذي لا يميل به الهوى فيجور في الحكم، وهو في الأصل مصدر سمي به فوضع موضع العادل، أو هو مصدر بمعنى العدالة، وهي الاعتدال والثبات على الحق، والمراد به عدالة الشهادة، وهي: وصف مركب معنى الإسلام، والبلوغ، والعقل، والحرية، وعدم الفسق بجارحة أو اعتقاد فخرج: غير المكلف كالصبي والمعتوه؛ لأنه قاصر عن القيام بالأمور على ما ينبغي، والعبد؛ لأنه مشغول بخدمة السيد لا يتفرغ للأمور، مستحقر في أعين الناس لا يهاب ولا يمثل أمره، وأما كونه ذكرا فهو مأخوذ من تذكير الوصف؛ فلا يكون الإمام امرأة ولا خنثى مشكلا؛ لأنه أشبه بالنساء الناقصات العقل والدين الممنوعات من الخروج، والفاسق لا يصلح لأمر الدين، ولا يوثق بأوامره ونواهيه، والظالم يختل به أمر الدين والدنيا؛ فلا يصلح للولاية.
وقد علم من قوله: «نصب» أن مستجمع شروط الإمامة الصالح لها لا يصير إماما بمجرد صلاحيته لها واستجماعه شروطها كما اتفق عليه الأئمة، بل لا بد من نص من الله، أو رسوله، أو من الإمام السابق.
كما أنه يؤخذ من قوله: «عدل» بصيغة الإفراد أنه لا يجوز تعدده في عصر وبلد واحد بالإجماع؛ لقوله: «من بايع إماما فأعطاه صفقة يده وثمرة قلبه، فليطعه إن استطاع، فإن جاء آخر ينازعه فاضربوا عنق...
From five conditions: And he described the imam by his saying: (just) and he is the one whom desire does not cause to incline so that he acts unjustly in judgment, and it is originally a verbal noun named with it, so it was placed in the position of 'the just', or it is a verbal noun meaning justice, and it is moderation and firmness upon truth, and what is intended by it is the justice of testimony, and it is a composite description meaning Islam, puberty, reason, freedom, and absence of sin by a limb or belief. So excluded: the non-obligated like the child and the insane, because he is incapable of undertaking affairs properly; and the slave, because he is occupied with the service of the master, not free for affairs, despised in the eyes of people, not feared nor his command obeyed. And as for his being male, it is taken from the masculine form of the description; so a woman cannot be imam, nor a hermaphrodite with ambiguity, because he resembles women who are deficient in intellect and religion, prevented from going out. And the sinner is not suitable for the matter of religion, nor is confidence placed in his commands and prohibitions. And the oppressor, the matter of religion and worldly affairs becomes corrupted by him; so he is not suitable for authority. And it is known from his saying: 'appointment' that the one who possesses the conditions of imamate, the one suitable for it, does not become imam merely by his suitability and his possessing its conditions, as the imams agreed; rather, there must be a text from God, or His Messenger, or from the previous imam. And it is also taken from his saying: 'just' in the singular form that it is not permissible for there to be multiple ones in one era and one land, by consensus, because of his saying: 'Whoever pledges allegiance to an imam and gives him the clasp of his hand and the fruit of his heart, let him obey him as much as he is able. Then if another comes to dispute with him, strike the neck of the other...'
Source pages p. 604

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for the general imamate (political leadership) in Islam. It begins by stating that the imam is described as 'just' (ʿadl), and then defines justice in detail. The author explains that 'adl here means the justice of testimony (ʿadālat al-shahāda), which is a composite quality comprising five elements: Islam, puberty (bulūgh), reason (ʿaql), freedom (ḥurriyya), and being free from sin (fisq) both in action and belief. The passage then excludes those who lack these conditions: the non-obligated (like children and the insane), slaves, women, and hermaphrodites. The reason given is functional: such persons are either incapable or not socially authoritative. The author also argues that mere qualification does not make one imam; rather, there must be an explicit appointment (naṣṣ) by God, the Prophet, or the previous imam. Finally, from the singular form of 'just', it is inferred that only one imam can exist at a time in a single region, citing a hadith about allegiance to one imam and killing any rival claimant.

Key Terms
الإمامة
العدل
الشروط
العدالة
الفِسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Justice (ʿadl) here is general moral uprightness.
Correction: It is a specific legal condition (ʿadālat al-shahāda) comprising five defined elements.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for the imam's justice?
A) Islam
B) Puberty
C) Wealth
D) Freedom

Key Takeaway
Justice for the imam is a legal composite of Islam, puberty, reason, freedom, and abstinence from sin, and excludes several categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the imam must be free from sin in action and belief, how do you justify following leaders who openly commit sins? What does that say about your own commitment to truth?
?
The passage says a woman cannot be imam because she is deficient in intellect and religion. Do you accept this as divine wisdom, or does it trouble you? How does your response reveal your own submission to revelation?
?
Justice (ʿadl) is defined as moderation and firmness upon truth. Where in your life are you swayed by desire rather than truth? Will you commit today to one specific area where you will be firm?

The Necessity of Appointment and Unity of the Imam
States the necessity of textual appointment and the impossibility of multiple imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that mere qualification does not make one imam; explicit appointment (naṣṣ) is required, and only one imam can exist at a time.

Core Idea
The imam is not self-appointed; he must be designated, and his singularity ensures unity.

After establishing the conditions of justice, Sanusi turns to two crucial procedural rules: how a person becomes imam and how many can hold the office simultaneously. He argues that possessing all qualifications does not automatically confer authority; rather, a clear designation (naṣṣ) from God, the Prophet, or the previous imam is necessary. He also derives from the singular form of 'just' that only one imam can lead the community at any given time, citing a strong prophetic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on appointment
Understand why mere suitability is insufficient – you must have a clear designation.
The term 'nāṣib' in the opening of the poem triggers this discussion.
2
Identify the sources of appointment
Recognize that designation can come from God, the Prophet, or the previous imam.
3
Examine the argument for singularity
How does the singular form of 'ʿadl' imply only one imam?
Sanusi also cites consensus and a hadith.
4
Reflect on the hadith
What does it mean to 'strike the neck of the other'? It indicates the seriousness of the unity.
The hadith is cut off in the source but its implication is clear.
5
Connect to broader theology
How this safeguards communal unity and prevents civil strife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من خمسة شروط:
ووصف الإمام بقوله: (عدل) وهو الذي لا يميل به الهوى فيجور في الحكم، وهو في الأصل مصدر سمي به فوضع موضع العادل، أو هو مصدر بمعنى العدالة، وهي الاعتدال والثبات على الحق، والمراد به عدالة الشهادة، وهي: وصف مركب معنى الإسلام، والبلوغ، والعقل، والحرية، وعدم الفسق بجارحة أو اعتقاد فخرج: غير المكلف كالصبي والمعتوه؛ لأنه قاصر عن القيام بالأمور على ما ينبغي، والعبد؛ لأنه مشغول بخدمة السيد لا يتفرغ للأمور، مستحقر في أعين الناس لا يهاب ولا يمثل أمره، وأما كونه ذكرا فهو مأخوذ من تذكير الوصف؛ فلا يكون الإمام امرأة ولا خنثى مشكلا؛ لأنه أشبه بالنساء الناقصات العقل والدين الممنوعات من الخروج، والفاسق لا يصلح لأمر الدين، ولا يوثق بأوامره ونواهيه، والظالم يختل به أمر الدين والدنيا؛ فلا يصلح للولاية.

كما أنه يؤخذ من قوله: «عدل» بصيغة الإفراد أنه لا يجوز تعدده في عصر وبلد واحد بالإجماع؛ لقوله: «من بايع إماما فأعطاه صفقة يده وثمرة قلبه، فليطعه إن استطاع، فإن جاء آخر ينازعه فاضربوا عنق...
From five conditions: And he described the imam by his saying: (just) and he is the one whom desire does not cause to incline so that he acts unjustly in judgment, and it is originally a verbal noun named with it, so it was placed in the position of 'the just', or it is a verbal noun meaning justice, and it is moderation and firmness upon truth, and what is intended by it is the justice of testimony, and it is a composite description meaning Islam, puberty, reason, freedom, and absence of sin by a limb or belief. So excluded: the non-obligated like the child and the insane, because he is incapable of undertaking affairs properly; and the slave, because he is occupied with the service of the master, not free for affairs, despised in the eyes of people, not feared nor his command obeyed. And as for his being male, it is taken from the masculine form of the description; so a woman cannot be imam, nor a hermaphrodite with ambiguity, because he resembles women who are deficient in intellect and religion, prevented from going out. And the sinner is not suitable for the matter of religion, nor is confidence placed in his commands and prohibitions. And the oppressor, the matter of religion and worldly affairs becomes corrupted by him; so he is not suitable for authority. And it is known from his saying: 'appointment' that the one who possesses the conditions of imamate, the one suitable for it, does not become imam merely by his suitability and his possessing its conditions, as the imams agreed; rather, there must be a text from God, or His Messenger, or from the previous imam. Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for the general imamate (political leadership) in Islam. It begins by stating that the imam is described as 'just' (ʿadl), and then defines justice in detail. The author explains that 'adl here means the justice of testimony (ʿadālat al-shahāda), which is a composite quality comprising five elements: Islam, puberty (bulūgh), reason (ʿaql), freedom (ḥurriyya), and being free from sin (fisq) both in action and belief. The passage then excludes those who lack these conditions: the non-obligated (like children and the insane), slaves, women, and hermaphrodites. The reason given is functional: such persons are either incapable or not socially authoritative. The author also argues that mere qualification does not make one imam; rather, there must be an explicit appointment (naṣṣ) by God, the Prophet, or the previous imam. Finally, from the singular form of 'just', it is inferred that only one imam can exist at a time in a single region, citing a hadith about allegiance to one imam and killing any rival claimant.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Explicit appointment by a recognized authority is necessary; suitability alone is not enough.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that suitability alone does not entitle anyone to leadership—divine appointment is necessary. In your own life, do you ever assume that your skills or achievements automatically qualify you for certain positions without seeking God's will? Will you sincerely submit to God's choice, even if it is not the one you think you deserve?
?
The hadith warns to obey the rightful imam and to strike down any rival claimant. Are you faithful to the legitimate authority in your community, or do you allow yourself to entertain disloyalty and division? If a dispute arises, are you quick to defend the unity of the community, or do you side with the challenger?
?
Unity of the imam is presented as essential. In your heart, are you truly united behind the leadership of your time, or do you harbor reservations that fracture your commitment? What step can you take today to strengthen your loyalty and contribute to the unity of the ummah?

Conditions of the Imam: Qualifications and Necessity of Designation
Sanusi disqualifies women, corrupt, and wrongdoers from imamate, then asserts necessity of designation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions required for an imam and why even a qualified person cannot become imam without explicit designation.

Core Idea
Fitness alone does not make an imam; divine or prophetic designation is essential.

In Sunni political theology, the imam must meet certain conditions, but meeting them is not enough. This passage from Sanusi explains who is disqualified and emphasizes that the imamate requires a specific designation from Allah, the Prophet, or the previous imam. Let's examine the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify disqualified candidates
The passage lists three categories of people unfit for imamate: women, the corrupt (fasiq), and the wrongdoer (zalim). Read the Arabic and translation to see how they are described.
Notice the strong language: 'deficient in intellect and religion' for women.
2
Understand why they are disqualified
Each disqualification is tied to a specific reason: women due to deficiencies, the corrupt due to untrustworthiness in religion, the wrongdoer due to disruption of both religion and worldly affairs. Reflect on how these reasons relate to the role of imam.
3
Grasp the necessity of designation
After listing disqualifications, Sanusi clarifies that even a fully qualified person does not automatically become imam. There must be a specific text (nass) designating him from Allah, the Prophet, or the previous imam. This is the key point of the lesson.
This is a consensus among the imams (scholars).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أشبه بالنِّساء النّاقصات العقل والدين الممنوعات من الخروج(١)، والفاسق لا يصلح لأمر الدِّين، ولا يُوثق بأوامره ونواهيه، والظَّالم يَختل به أمر الدين والدنيا؛ فلا يصلح للولاية. وقد علم مِن قوله: «نصب» أنَّ مُستجمعَ شُروطِ الإمامةِ الصَّالحُ لها لا يصيرُ إمامًا بمُجرَّدِ صلاحيّته لها واستجماعه شروطها كما اتفق عليه الأئمة، بل لا بُدَّ مِن نصّ مِن اللهِ (۲)، أو رَسوله (۳)، أو من الإمام السابق (٤).
Similar to women who are deficient in intellect and religion, prevented from leaving (without permission), and the corrupt person is not suitable for the matter of religion, nor are his commands and prohibitions trusted, and the wrongdoer corrupts the matter of religion and worldly life; so he is not suitable for leadership. And it is known from His statement: 'designation' that one who possesses all conditions of the imamate and is fit for it does not become imam merely by his fitness and possession of its conditions, as the imams have agreed, but rather there must be a designation from Allah, or His Messenger, or from the previous imam.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions of the imam and the necessity of designation. It begins by comparing unqualified candidates: women are described as deficient in religion and intellect and are normally prohibited from leaving home unescorted, so they are not fit. Similarly, a corrupt person (fasiq) cannot be trusted with religious orders, and a wrongdoer (zalim) disrupts both religion and worldly affairs. Therefore, none of these are suitable for the imamate. The passage then clarifies that even someone who meets all the required conditions does not automatically become imam. Rather, there must be a specific designation (nass) from Allah (e.g., for Dawud), from His Messenger (e.g., for Abu Bakr), or from the previous imam (e.g., Abu Bakr for Umar). This is the consensus of the scholars.

Key Terms
نصب
إمامة
فاسق
ظالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who meets all conditions automatically becomes imam.
Correction: The passage states that even a fully qualified person does not become imam without explicit designation from Allah, His messenger, or the previous imam.

Checkpoint
What is required for a person to become imam beyond possessing the conditions?
A) Consensus of the people
B) Designation from Allah, His Messenger, or previous imam
C) Popular election
D) Being the most pious

Key Takeaway
The imamate requires both personal qualifications and explicit designation; fitness alone is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that someone's knowledge or piety alone makes them fit to lead you spiritually? This lesson shows that even a fully qualified person does not become imam without designation. Who are you following as a guide in your faith? Have you verified that they have legitimate authority, or have you simply assumed based on their outward qualities? What concrete step can you take today to investigate the legitimacy of those you look up to?
?
The passage disqualifies the fasiq and zalim from leadership. Look honestly at the people you admire or obey in your community. Are there any signs of corruption or wrongdoing in their lives that you have ignored? How does your conscience respond to this lesson? Are you willing to distance yourself from such figures even if it means losing status or relationships? Make a commitment to seek leaders who are truly just and trustworthy.
?
Designation in this lesson comes only from Allah, His Messenger, or the previous imam. This means religious leadership is not a personal ambition. Have you ever sought a position of authority or influence in your religious community? What was your intention? This lesson calls you to examine your heart: do you desire leadership for the sake of Allah, or for personal pride and control? Be honest with yourself and repent if needed, then commit to only seeking roles that come through proper channels and with pure intention.

Permissibility of Multiple Imams in One Era and Territory
Derivation and proof for multiple imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the singular form of 'adl' implies the permissibility of multiple imams and the evidence from hadith for this permissibility.

Core Idea
The singular 'adl' does not prevent multiple rightful imams, but only one is obeyed.

The text begins by deriving a legal ruling from the word 'adl' (just) in the singular form. You might wonder why this matters for the imamate. The author shows that the singular form does not preclude multiplicity, and then supports this with a hadith commanding obedience to one imam and striking any challenger.

Guided Path
1
Understand the derivation
Notice that the author derives permissibility of multiple imams from the singular 'adl'. Reflect on the logic: if 'adl' were meant to indicate only one, the command to strike challengers would be unnecessary.
The derivation is based on the absence of explicit limitation.
2
Examine the hadith proof
Read the hadith: it commands obedience to the imam one has pledged allegiance to, and if another disputes, strike his neck. This shows that only one imam is recognized, but the singular 'adl' does not forbid multiple potential imams.
3
Reflect on consensus
The passage states this permissibility is by consensus (ijma). Consider that this consensus is based on the hadith and the practice of the Sahaba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما أنَّه يؤخذ من قوله: «عدل» بصيغة الإفراد يجوز تعدده ده في عصر وبلدٍ واحد (ه) بالإجماع؛ لقوله: «من بايع إماما فأعطاه صفقة يده وثمرة قلبه (٦)، فليطعه إن استطاع، فإن جاء آخر ينازعه فاضربوا عنق الآخر» (۱) (۲) وفي رواية: «فاضربوه بالسَّيف كائنا من كان» (۳). ثم المراد من كونه عدلاً أي: ولو ظاهرا (٤) عند النَّصب؛ لأنَّه الَّذِي كُلّفنا به، وهذه شرط (ه) في الابتداء(٦) وحالة الاختيار، وقوله (بِالشَّرْعِ) مُتعلّق بـ: «وَاجِبٌ» وهو المقصود بالإفادة(٧)، يعني: أنَّ وجوب نصب الإمام على الأُمَّة طريقه الشرع عند أهل السُّنَّةِ وجمهور المعتزلة؛ لوجوه (۸) عُمدتها (۹): إجماعُ الصَّحابة رضي الله تعالى عنهم، حتّى جعلوه أهم الواجبات، واشتغلوا به عن دفن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، وكذا عَقِبَ موتِ كل إمام إلى وقتنا هذا.
And also it is taken from His statement: 'just' in the singular form that it is permissible for there to be multiple [imams] in one era and one territory by consensus; because of his saying: 'Whoever pledges allegiance to an imam and gives him his handshake and the fruit of his heart, let him obey him as much as he can; if another comes to dispute with him, strike the neck of the other.' And in another narration: 'Strike him with the sword, whoever he may be.' Then the intended meaning of 'just' is: even if apparently at the time of appointment, because that is what we are charged with, and this is a condition at the beginning and in a state of choice. And His statement 'by the Shari'a' is attached to 'obligatory', and it is what is intended to be conveyed, meaning that the obligation of appointing an imam upon the community, its source is the Shari'a according to the Ahl al-Sunna and the majority of the Mu'tazila, due to aspects, the main of which is the consensus of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, to the point that they made it the most important of obligations and occupied themselves with it over burying the Prophet, and likewise after the death of every imam until our time.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage addresses several related issues. First, it derives from the word 'just' in the singular form that it is permissible to have multiple imams in one era and one territory, according to consensus. This is proven by the hadith that commands obedience to the imam one has pledged allegiance to, and if another challenges him, the challenger should be killed. This implies that only one imam is recognized at a time, but the singular form of 'just' does not preclude multiplicity. Then it clarifies that the condition of 'just' means apparent justice at the time of appointment, which is what we are charged with, and it is a condition at the beginning, not necessarily continuance. Finally, it states that the obligation to appoint an imam is derived from the Shari'a, not reason, as evidenced by the consensus of the Sahaba who prioritized appointing a leader over burying the Prophet. This consensus is the main proof for Ahl al-Sunna and most Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
عدل
نصب الإمام
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the singular 'adl' implies there can only be one imam at a time, and that multiple imams are forbidden.
Correction: The singular form does not forbid multiplicity; rather, the hadith indicates that only one imam is to be obeyed, but multiple just individuals could exist simultaneously.

Checkpoint
What does the author derive from the singular form of 'adl'?
A) That only one imam can exist
B) That multiple imams are permissible
C) That imams must be just
D) That imams are chosen by consensus

Key Takeaway
The singular 'adl' does not prohibit multiple imams; the hadith about striking the challenger shows only one is obeyed but multiple may be just.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly wished for a different leader or imam because you disagreed with some decisions? How does the Prophet's command to strike the challenger challenge that desire? Will you consciously submit to the authority you have pledged allegiance to, even when your heart pulls elsewhere?
?
If you were to meet a just and qualified imam other than your own, would you be tempted to give him your allegiance? How does this lesson's teaching about multiple imams but only one obedience settle that conflict? Are you ready to prioritize loyalty over personal preference?
?
When you see division and disputes in the Muslim community over leadership, does your soul rush to take sides? This passage commands striking the challenger—not because the challenger is unjust, but to preserve unity. Are you willing to 'strike down' your own partisan feelings for the sake of the community's unity?

The Condition of Apparent Justice in the Imam
Clarification of justice condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the required justice for an imam is only apparent (zahir) and is a condition at the time of appointment, not necessarily afterward.

Core Idea
Only apparent justice is required at the time of appointment.

After discussing multiplicity, the author clarifies an important point about the justice condition. You might think the imam must be truly just in his inner state, but the text explains that we are only charged with apparent justice, and it is a condition at the beginning, not throughout.

Guided Path
1
Identify the clarification
The phrase 'even if apparently' indicates that the justice required is outward, not necessarily inward. This is what we are commanded to assess.
2
Note the timing condition
The condition applies at the time of appointment (al-nasb) and in a state of choice. It does not require the imam to remain just forever.
This limits the obligation of the community to assess justice only initially.
3
Reflect on the wisdom
Why only apparent? Because we cannot know the unseen. This aligns with the practical nature of Shari'a rulings.

The Basis of Obligation: Consensus of the Companions
Proof for obligation from Shari'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the obligation to appoint an imam is established by Shari'a, not reason, and the main evidence is the consensus of the Sahaba.

Core Idea
The obligation to appoint an imam comes from Shari'a, proven by consensus.

The last part of the passage addresses a fundamental question: why is it obligatory to have an imam? Some rationalist groups claimed reason alone suffices, but the author here aligns with Ahl al-Sunna and most Mu'tazila, stating the obligation is from Shari'a. The primary proof is the consensus of the Companions, who prioritized it over even burying the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Link the phrase
The author connects 'by the Shari'a' to 'obligatory' to indicate the source of obligation. Notice this is the intended meaning.
2
Identify the main proof
The main evidence is the consensus (ijma) of the Sahaba. They considered appointing an imam so important that they occupied themselves with it before burying the Prophet.
This shows the obligation is from Shari'a because the Sahaba acted on it as a religious duty.
3
Consider the implication
This consensus continues after every imam's death until our time. Reflect on the continuity of this obligation.

Conditions of the Imamate and Obligation by Sharia
Sanusi defines conditions for the Imam and proves the obligation by Sharia through Ijma'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions required for an Imam and the evidence that the obligation to appoint him is established by Sharia, not reason.

Core Idea
The Imamate is a divinely mandated obligation, proven by the consensus of the Companions.

This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid commentary clarifies the foundational rulings of political leadership in Islam. It explains that the obligation to appoint an Imam is rooted in divine law, not rational necessity, and outlines key conditions such as justice. The author also highlights the decisive proof from the consensus of the Companions, who prioritized the appointment over the burial of the Prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage slowly, then follow with the literal translation. Let the author's own words establish the framework before you analyze.
Notice how the passage first mentions conditions, then shifts to the proof of obligation.
2
Identify the conditions of the Imam
Look for the condition of justice ('adalah) and the nuance that it is required outwardly at the time of appointment. Understand that this applies at the beginning and when there is a choice.
The hadith 'Strike him with the sword' indicates that justice is a critical condition.
3
Understand the proof for obligation
Focus on the argument that the obligation is from Sharia. The author cites the consensus (ijma') of the Companions as the strongest proof. Reflect on how their actions—prioritizing the appointment over the Prophet's burial—demonstrate its importance.
Disagreement over who should be caliph does not undermine the agreement on the obligation itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شروط الإمامة العامة وفي كونه عدلاً ، رواية: «فاضربوه بالسَّيف كائنا من كان» ثم المراد من أي : ولو ظاهرا عند النَّصب ؛ لأنَّه الَّذِي كُلّفنا به، وهذه شرط في الابتداء وحالة الاختيار ، وقوله (بِالشَّرْعِ) مُتعلّق بـ: «وَاجِبٌ» وهو المقصود بالإفادة ، يعني: أنَّ وجوب نصب الإمام على الأُمَّة طريقه الشرع عند أهل السُّنَّةِ وجمهور المعتزلة؛ لوجوه عُمدتها: إجماعُ الصَّحابة رضي الله تعالى عنهم ، حتّى جعلوه أهم الواجبات ، واشتغلوا به عن دفن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وكذا عَقِبَ موتِ كل إمام إلى وقتنا هذا. واختلافهم في تعيين مَن يصلُح خليفة غير قادح في اتفاقهم على وجوب نصبه ، ولذا لم يقل أحد منهم : لا حاجة إلى الإمام. وكَمَّل البيتَ بقوله: (فَاعْلَمْ).
Conditions of the general imamate. And concerning him being just, a narration: 'Strike him with the sword, whoever he may be.' Then the intended meaning is: even if apparent, at the time of appointment, because it is what we are charged with. And this is a condition at the beginning and in a state of choice. And His statement (by the Sharia) is attached to 'obligatory', and it is the intended benefit, meaning: that the obligation of appointing the Imam upon the community is by way of the Sharia according to Ahl al-Sunnah and the majority of the Mu'tazila, due to proofs, the strongest of which is the consensus of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, until they made it the most important of obligations and occupied themselves with it over burying the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and likewise after the death of every Imam until our time. And their disagreement over appointing who is suitable as a caliph does not harm their agreement on the obligation of his appointment, so none of them said: There is no need for an Imam. And he completed the verse with his statement: 'So know.'
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage is an exposition defining conditions for the Imam and proving that the obligation to appoint him is established by the Sharia, not by reason alone. It begins by stating conditions: the Imam must be just, as indicated by the hadith 'Strike him with the sword', and this justice is only required outwardly at the time of appointment, since we are only charged with the apparent. This condition applies at the beginning of the imamate and when there is a choice. Then the author explains that the phrase 'by the Sharia' (in the poem) is attached to 'obligatory', meaning that the obligation of appointing an Imam is known through divine law, not rational necessity. This is the position of Ahl al-Sunnah and the majority of the Mu'tazila. The proof is the consensus (ijma') of the Companions, who prioritized appointing a successor over burying the Prophet, showing they considered it the most important obligation. This practice continued after every Imam. The fact that they disagreed on who should be the caliph does not invalidate their agreement on the obligation itself; no Companion ever claimed there was no need for an Imam. The passage concludes by noting that the poet completed the verse with 'so know', indicating the certainty of this ruling.

Correction: The passage asserts that the obligation is based on Sharia, specifically the consensus of the Companions, not mere reason.

Checkpoint

The appointment of an Imam is an obligation established by Sharia, as demonstrated by the unanimous practice of the Companions.

Imamate as a Fiqhi Matter, Not a Foundational Belief
Refutation of Mu'tazila on imamate's rational obligation and clarification of its fiqhi nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation of the Imamate is a matter of Sharia, not reason, and why denying it does not necessarily constitute disbelief.

Core Idea
The Imamate is a fiqhi duty, not a creedal pillar; denial is not automatically disbelief.

The passage begins by refuting the Mu'tazila who claimed that the obligation to appoint an Imam is known by reason alone. It then clarifies the status of the Imamate: it is not a foundational belief like the Five Pillars of Islam, which are known by necessity and agreed upon. Therefore, denying it does not automatically make one a disbeliever. Finally, it commands obedience to the Imam, provided his commands are clear and operative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then its translation. Notice how the text first refutes the Mu'tazila and then explains the status of the Imamate.
The passage is commentary on a verse from Jawharat al-Tawhid.
2
Identify the refutation
Find where the author refutes the Mu'tazila. What is their position? How does the author respond?
The key phrase is 'not by the judgment of reason.'
3
Understand the classification
The author says the Imamate is not a 'rukn' (pillar) like the Five Pillars. What does this mean for its status in belief and practice?
Pillars are known by necessity and transmitted via tawatur.
4
Note the condition for takfir
The passage states that deniers of the Imamate are not automatically disbelievers. Under what condition might they be?
The condition is if the matter is known by necessity from religion, which the Imamate is not.
5
Reflect on obedience
The passage ends with a command to obey the Imam. How does this fit with the earlier points?
Obedience is to his clear commands, not blind obedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأراد بقوله: (لَا بِحُكْمِ العَقْلِ الرَّدَّ على بعض المعتزلة حيث ذهبوا إلى أن وجوب نصب الإمام ليس بالشرع. فَلَيْسَ رُكْنًا يُعْتَقَدْ فِي الدِّينِ وَلَا تَزِغْ عَنْ أَمْرِهِ المُبِينِ (فَلَيْسَ) نصبُ الإمام (رُكْنًا يُعْتَقَد) وجوبًا (في الدِّينِ) متعلق بـ(ركنا)، أي: لا تتوهم من ذكري له في القواعد الكلامية أنَّه من القواعد المُجمع عليها المنقولة بالتّواتر كالشَّهادتين والصلاة والزَّكاة وصوم رمضان والحج ، بل ليس هو منها ، وكلُّ ما ليس كذلك فحكمه حكم سائر الشَّرعيّات يجب اعتقاد ما صح منها ، ولا يكفر منكره إلَّا إذا وُجِد شرطه السابق (وَلَا تَزِغْ أي: لا تخرج (عَنْ) امتثالِ أَمْرِهِ) ونهيه (المُبِينِ) أي: الواضح الجاري
And He intended by His statement: 'Not by the judgment of reason' as a refutation of some Mu'tazila who held that the obligation of appointing the Imam is not by the Sharia. 'So it is not a pillar to be believed in the religion, and do not deviate from his clear command.' ('So it is not') the appointment of the Imam ('a pillar to be believed') as an obligation ('in the religion') is attached to ('a pillar'), meaning: do not imagine, from my mentioning it among the theological principles, that it is one of the principles agreed upon and transmitted via tawatur like the two testimonies, prayer, zakat, fasting Ramadan, and Hajj. Rather, it is not from them. And everything that is not like that, its ruling is the ruling of other Sharia matters: one must believe what is authentic of them, and its denier is not deemed a disbeliever unless its prior condition exists. ('And do not deviate') meaning: do not depart from compliance with his command and prohibition ('the clear') meaning: the apparent and operative.
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and clarification. It first explains that the poet's phrase 'not by the judgment of reason' is directed against some Mu'tazila (like al-Jahiz) who argued that the obligation to appoint an Imam is known by reason, not revelation. The author rejects this, affirming it is by Sharia. Then the passage addresses the status of the Imamate: it is not a foundational belief (rukn) like the Five Pillars of Islam (shahada, prayer, zakat, fasting, Hajj). These are transmitted via tawatur and agreed upon by consensus. Denying them leads to disbelief. The Imamate, however, is not of that category. Its ruling is like other Sharia-based obligations: one must believe in what is authentically established, but its denier is not automatically deemed a disbeliever unless the prior condition is met—namely, that the issue is agreed upon and known by necessity from religion. Since the Imamate's obligation, though agreed upon by Ahl al-Sunnah and even the Mu'tazila, is not known by necessity (like the pillars), denial does not entail takfir. Finally, the passage commands obedience to the Imam: do not deviate from his clear command and prohibition, meaning his orders that are apparent and operative in the community.

Key Terms
إمامة
ركن
تكفير
معتزلة
شرع
عقل
طاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any denial of the Imamate is disbelief.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the Imamate is not a foundational pillar known by necessity, so denial does not automatically cause disbelief; additional conditions are required for takfir.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage say the denier of the Imamate is not automatically a disbeliever?
A) Because the Imamate is not obligatory
B) Because the Imamate is not known by necessity from religion like the Five Pillars
C) Because all scholars agree it is a fiqhi issue
D) Because the Mu'tazila do not believe in it

Key Takeaway
The Imamate is a Sharia-based obligation, not a creedal pillar; its denial does not automatically cause disbelief unless the matter is known by necessity from religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered someone less of a Muslim because of their position on imamate or other secondary issues? How does this lesson challenge you to reassess that tendency and to only apply takfīr where the Sharia clearly demands it?

?
When you hear a command from a leader or scholar, do you immediately obey, or do you evaluate whether it is a 'clear command' as the passage describes? What would it take for you to deviate from a command you believe is not supported by clear evidence?

Imamah: A Fiqhi Obligation, Not a Creedal Pillar
The author clarifies the fiqhi nature of Imamah and the duty of obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imamah is considered a fiqhi issue within the Ahl al-Sunnah tradition and how its denial differs from denying core pillars of Islam.

Core Idea
Imamah is a fiqhi obligation, not a universal creedal pillar.

The author of Jawharat al-Tawhid discusses Imamah within a kalam work but immediately clarifies its status. He warns that despite its inclusion, it is not among the fundamentals transmitted by tawatur. Instead, it is a shar'i ruling like other fiqhi matters. This lesson unpacks that important distinction and the obligation of obedience to the Imam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the literal English translation to understand the author's core clarification.
Notice the author's double move: he first negates Imamah as a pillar, then asserts obedience.
2
Identify the two key claims
Identify where the author states that Imamah is not a pillar (rukn) like the five pillars, and where he commands obedience to the Imam. Underline the textual evidence.
The first claim is about status; the second is about obligation.
3
Reflect on the evidence for obedience
Consider the Quranic verse (4:59) and the hadith cited. Why does the author include these? What do they establish about the nature of obedience?
Obedience is both outward and inward, based on revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَلَيْسَ رُكْنًا يُعْتَقَدْ فِي الدِّينِ وَلَا تَزِغْ عَنْ أَمْرِهِ المُبِينِ
(فَلَيْسَ) نصبُ الإمام (رُكْنًا يُعْتَقَد) وجوبًا (في .الدِّينِ) متعلق بـ(ركنا)، أي: لا تتوهم من ذكري له في القواعد الكلامية أنَّه من القواعد المُجمع عليها المنقولة بالتّواتر كالشَّهادتين والصلاة والزَّكاة وصوم رمضان والحج ، بل ليس هو منها ، وكلُّ ما ليس كذلك فحكمه حكم سائر الشَّرعيّات يجب اعتقاد ما صح منها ، ولا يكفر منكره إلَّا إذا وُجِد شرطه السابق (وَلَا تَزِغْ أي: لا تخرج (عَنْ) امتثالِ أَمْرِهِ) ونهيه (المُبِينِ) أي: الواضح الجاري على قوانين الشرع ، ولا عن أمر خُلفائه ونُوَّابِه؛ لأنَّ طاعته واجبةٌ على جميع الرّعايا بالظاهر والباطن؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿يَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا أَطِيعُوا اللَّهَ وَأَطِيعُوا الرَّسُولَ وَأُولِي الْأَمْرِ مِنكُمْ﴾ [النساء: ٥٩]، ولقوله : «من أطاع أميري مِنكُمْ فقد أطاعني، ومَن عصى أميري فقد عصاني» فلا تجوز مخالفته .
So it is not a pillar to be believed in the religion, and do not deviate from his clear command. ('So it is not' means: the appointment of the Imam is not a pillar to be believed as obligatory in the religion. That is, do not imagine from my mention of it in the theological principles that it is among the universally agreed-upon principles transmitted by tawatur, like the two testimonies, prayer, zakat, fasting Ramadan, and Hajj. Rather, it is not among them, and everything that is not like that has the same ruling as other shar'i matters: one must believe in what is soundly established among them, and its denier is not deemed a disbeliever unless its prior condition is found. And 'do not deviate' means: do not go out from compliance with his command and his clear prohibition, i.e., the clear one proceeding according to the laws of the Shari'a, nor from the command of his deputies and representatives; because obedience to him is obligatory upon all subjects outwardly and inwardly, due to His saying, the Most High: 'O you who have believed, obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' [Quran 4:59], and due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever obeys my commander has obeyed me, and whoever disobeys my commander has disobeyed me.' So it is not permissible to oppose him.)
Source pages p. 607

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a couplet from the poem. It provides a definition and classification of the status of Imamah. The author begins by clarifying that the appointment of the Imam is not a fundamental pillar of belief (rukn) like the obligatory nature of the five pillars of Islam. He warns the reader not to misunderstand his inclusion of the topic in a work of kalam as implying that it is among the matters of tawatur and universal consensus. Instead, it is a fiqhi matter: its ruling is like other shar'i rulings—one must believe in it if it is authentically established, but denying it does not entail disbelief unless the conditions for takfir (i.e., the matter being unanimously recognized and known by necessity from the religion) are met. The author then moves to the next part of the couplet, which commands obedience to the Imam. He explains that obedience is obligatory both outwardly and inwardly, and provides textual evidence from the Quran (verse 4:59) and a hadith. The obedience is to the Imam and his deputies, and it is not permissible to disobey him. The passage thus establishes the nature of Imamah as a fiqhi obligation and the duty of obedience.

Correction: The author clarifies that Imamah is not among the fundamentals transmitted by tawatur; it is a fiqhi obligation. Its denial does not automatically constitute disbelief unless the matter is known by necessity from the religion.

The Imam Is Not Deposed Except for Clear Kufr
Sanusi states the sole condition for deposing an Imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that an Imam cannot be deposed except for clear kufr, and the theological reasoning behind this rule.

Core Idea
An Imam is only removed for clear disbelief, not for any sin or injustice.

The relationship between the ruler and the ruled in Islam is one of obedience, but it is not absolute. Sunni scholars have established a clear boundary: the Imam may only be deposed if he commands clear kufr. This passage from Sanusi's poem and its commentary concisely states that rule. Let's examine the precise condition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic couplet and translation
Start by reading the Arabic couplet and its literal translation. Notice the strong negative construction 'لا يُعزَلُ ... إلا' which limits deposition to one condition.
The couplet is from a longer poem; here it is presented as a standalone ruling.
2
Note the key condition: clear kufr
The commentary specifies that the kufr must be 'clear' (صريح). Understand that this excludes ambiguous actions or minor sins. The Imam must command explicit disbelief.
This distinguishes the Sunni position from those who allow rebellion for major sins.
3
Reflect on the consequences
Consider the instruction 'cast aside his covenant' and rely on Allah. This implies that rebellion is not to be undertaken lightly; only when the Imam's kufr is manifest should obedience be withdrawn.
The reliance on Allah indicates that the community should not engage in armed revolt but rather disassociate and trust in divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الا يُعزَلُ الإمامُ إِلَّا بِكُفره
١٣٢- إِلَّا بِكُفْرِ فَانْبِذَنَّ عَهْدَهُ فَالله يَكْفِينَا أذاهُ وَحْدَهُ
(إِلَّا) إذا أَمَرَ بِكُفْرِ (صريح).
The Imam is not deposed except for his kufr. Except with kufr, so cast aside his covenant, and Allah alone will suffice us against his harm. (Except) if he commands clear kufr.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) on the deposition of the Imam. The couplet states that the Imam is not removed from office except for kufr (disbelief). The commentary specifies that 'except' means if he commands clear kufr. The implication is that the Imam should not be deposed due to injustice, tyranny, or sinfulness, even major sins, as long as it does not amount to kufr. The phrasing 'فَانْبِذَنَّ عَهْدَهُ' means to cast aside the covenant of obedience to him, followed by a reliance on Allah to deal with his harm. This is in line with the Sunni position that rebellion against a Muslim ruler is prohibited unless he openly commits kufr.

Key Terms
عزل
إمام
كفر
عهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that an Imam may be deposed for any major sin or injustice.
Correction: The passage clearly limits deposition to kufr (disbelief), and specifically clear kufr commanded by the Imam. Sins, even grave ones, do not justify rebellion.

Checkpoint
Based on the passage, under what condition may an Imam be deposed?
A) If he commits any major sin.
B) If he is unjust in his rule.
C) Only if he commands clear kufr.
D) If the people unanimously decide to depose him.

Key Takeaway
The sole condition for deposing an Imam is his command of clear kufr; no other sin justifies removal.

The Imam is Not Removed Except for Kufr
The commentator states the rule of imamate, its exception, and provides textual evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to Sunni theology, the imam is only removed for clear disbelief (kufr) and that obedience to him is obligatory based on Quranic and prophetic proofs.

Core Idea
Obedience to the imam is binding unless he commits clear disbelief.

This lesson presents a foundational principle of Sunni political theology: the imam’s authority is binding, and removal is only permitted for apostasy. We will examine the textual proofs that establish this rule, drawing from the Quran and the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise legal style and the scriptural citations.
The passage begins with a clear rule and then supports it with a Quranic verse and a hadith.
2
Identify the rule and its exception
The rule is that the imam is not removed from office. The only exception mentioned is his committing kufr. Note that the exception is clarified to be according to the Sharia, meaning it is not for minor sins.
The commentator also clarifies that this rule applies to the imam’s deputies and representatives.
3
Reflect on the proofs
Two proofs are given: the Quranic verse (4:59) commanding obedience to 'those in authority' and a hadith equating obedience to the amir with obedience to the Prophet. Reflect on how these establish the obligation of outward and inward obedience.
The passage concludes that opposing the imam in lawful matters is not permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يُعزل الإمام إلَّا بكفره (٢) على قوانين الشرع (۱) ، ولا عن أمر خُلفائه ونُوَّابِه (۲) ؛ لأنَّ طاعته واجبةٌ على جميع الرّعايا بالظاهر والباطن (۳) ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿يَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا أَطِيعُوا اللَّهَ وَأَطِيعُوا الرَّسُولَ وَأُولِي الْأَمْرِ مِنكُمْ) (٤) [النساء: ٥٩] ، ولقوله : «من أطاع أميري مِنكُمْ فقد أطاعني(٥) ، ومَن عصى أميري فقد عصاني»(٦) فلا تجوز مخالفته .
The imam is not removed except for his kufr (2) according to the laws of the Sharia (1), and not concerning the command of his deputies and representatives (2); because obedience to him is obligatory upon all subjects outwardly and inwardly (3); due to His statement, the Exalted: 'O you who have believed, obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' (4) [An-Nisa 4:59], and due to his statement: 'Whoever obeys my amir from among you has obeyed me (5), and whoever disobeys my amir has disobeyed me' (6). So opposing him is not permissible.
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by stating the rule that the imam is not removed from office except for his kufr (disbelief). It clarifies that this removal is according to the laws of the Sharia, meaning it is not for any sin short of apostasy. It then adds that one should not disobey the commands of the imam's deputies and representatives. The reason given is that obedience to the imam is obligatory upon all subjects both outwardly (in action) and inwardly (in intention). The commentator provides two proofs: first, the Quranic verse 4:59 which commands obedience to Allah, the Messenger, and those in authority (the imam and scholars/rulers); second, a hadith that equates obeying the amir to obeying the Prophet and disobeying the amir to disobeying the Prophet. Therefore, opposing the imam in lawful matters is not permissible.

Key Terms
الإمام
الكفر
طاعة الإمام
أولو الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the imam can be removed for any major sin or injustice.
Correction: The passage explicitly states the imam is only removed for kufr (disbelief) and according to Sharia, not for other sins.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the only valid reason for removing an imam?
A) Any major sin
B) Kufr (disbelief)
C) Injustice
D) Neglect of duties

Key Takeaway
The imam is only removed for kufr, and obedience to him is obligatory on all believers outwardly and inwardly, based on clear Quranic and prophetic evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that the imam is not to be removed except for clear kufr. Have you ever spoken against a Muslim leader or teacher because of a personal disagreement, rather than because of something that contradicts the fundamentals of faith? How will you guard your tongue from unjust criticism?
?
Obedience to the imam is required 'outwardly and inwardly'. Is your obedience to those in authority over you (parents, teachers, rulers) ever only for show, while your heart harbors resentment or rebellion? What step will you take today to make your inner state match your outer compliance?
?
The Prophet ﷺ said that obeying the amir is obeying him, and disobeying the amir is disobeying him. When you disobey a legitimate authority in a lawful matter, you are in effect disobeying the Prophet himself. How does this realization change the way you view your responsibilities toward those in charge? Will you seek forgiveness for past disobedience and commit to sincere obedience going forward?
