Dealing with an Imam Who Commands Kufr
The commentator explains the exception when the imam commands kufr and the graduated response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the exception to obedience when an imam commands kufr and the appropriate response based on one's ability and fear of harm.

Core Idea
When the imam commands kufr, allegiance is void; respond according to ability.

This lesson clarifies a critical exception in the obligation to obey the imam. While obedience is generally required, if the imam commands explicit or implicit kufr, that obedience is no longer permissible. However, the response is not one-size-fits-all: the believer must weigh the risk of harm and act accordingly, either openly renouncing or secretly dissociating until able to remove the imam.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the exception
Read the opening phrase 'Except for kufr'. Note that this is the only legitimate reason to break allegiance. Understand that kufr can be explicit (e.g., declaring Allah to be a body) or implicit (e.g., commanding prostration to an idol).
The exception is strict: only kufr justifies dissolution of the covenant.
2
Assess the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between fear of death and no fear. If you fear being killed based on circumstantial evidence, you may outwardly comply (taqiyya) while inwardly rejecting. If you do not fear death and are able, you must openly renounce the covenant.
The ruling is not absolute; it considers the believer's safety.
3
Reliance on Allah
The final part of the passage reminds us that Allah alone suffices against the harm of the unjust imam. This is a call to spiritual reliance and trust that Allah controls all affairs.
Even when unable to act openly, the believer puts trust in Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِلَّا بِكُفْرِ فَانْبِذَنَّ عَهْدَهُ فَالله يَكْفِينَا أذاهُ وَحْدَهُ (إِلَّا) إذا أَمَرَ بِكُفْرِ صريح أو ضمني (۱) ؛ فلا تجوز طاعتُه إِلَّا إِنْ خِفْتَ القتل بقرائنِ الأحوال، فإن لم تخف القتل (۲) وقدرت على طرح عَهدِهِ (فَانْبِذَنَّ) أي: فاطرحنَّ (عَهْدَهُ) وبيعته جَهرة لكُفره المُوجب لانخلاعه (۳) عَن استحقاق التَّوفية له ؛ إذ لم يجعل الله للكافرين على المؤمنين سبيلا ، فإنْ لم تَقدِرْ على الجَهر بذلك فاطرَحْهُ سرًّا حتّى تجدَ قُدرةَ القيام بخلْعِه (فَاللَّهُ يَكْفِينَا أَذَاهُ) أي الجائر الذي أمر بالكفر وتلبس به . (وَحْدَهُ) إِذ هو (وَحْدَهُ) إذ هو الذي ناصيته بيد قدرته (٤)
Except for kufr, so cast aside his covenant, and Allah suffices us from his harm alone. (Except) if he commands explicit or implicit kufr (1); then obedience to him is not permissible unless you fear being killed based on circumstantial evidence. If you do not fear being killed (2) and are able to discard his covenant, then (cast aside) meaning: cast aside his covenant and his allegiance openly, because of his kufr that necessitates his being stripped (3) of the entitlement to fulfillment for him; since Allah has not given the disbelievers any path over the believers. If you are not able to do so openly, then discard it secretly until you find the ability to undertake removing him. (Allah suffices us from his harm), i.e., the unjust one who commanded kufr and was tainted with it. (Alone), for He is (alone), for He is the one whose forelock is in His power (4).
Source pages p. 608

Commentary
This passage explains the exception to the rule of not removing the imam. It begins by quoting the poem line: 'Except for kufr, so cast aside his covenant, and Allah suffices us alone from his harm.' The commentator then explains: 'Except' means if the imam commands explicit kufr (like declaring Allah to be a body or denying a known religious necessity) or implicit kufr (like commanding prostration to an idol). In such cases, obedience to him is not permissible. However, there is a concession: if you fear being killed based on circumstantial evidence (e.g., the imam's known cruelty), you may outwardly comply to save your life, but inwardly you must reject. If you do not fear death and you are able to discard his covenant (the pledge of allegiance), then you must openly renounce your allegiance to him. The reason is that his kufr strips him of the right to your fulfillment of the pledge, because Allah has decreed that disbelievers have no authority over believers (Quran 4:141). If you are unable to do so openly due to fear of harm, then you should secretly break the allegiance until you find the strength to remove him. The poem's closing 'Allah suffices us from his harm' means that the believing community relies on Allah to deal with the unjust imam who committed kufr. 'Alone' emphasizes that Allah alone controls his destiny, as 'his forelock is in His power' (an allusion to Quran 11:56).

Key Terms
الكفر
العهد
البيعة
الخوف
القتل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any sin of the imam justifies rebellion.
Correction: This passage clarifies that only kufr (explicit or implicit) justifies breaking allegiance, and even then, the response is measured: open renunciation only when safe, otherwise secret dissociation.

Checkpoint
What should a believer do if the imam commands explicit kufr and the believer fears being killed?
A) Obey the command
B) Immediately rebel openly
C) Obey outwardly but reject inwardly
D) Leave the land

Key Takeaway
When an imam commands kufr, his covenant is void; believers must respond according to their ability, openly renouncing if safe, or secretly dissociating if in danger, relying on Allah to remove his harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were under a ruler who commanded you to do something that is kufr, would you have the courage to openly renounce your allegiance, even at great personal cost? What would hold you back?
?
Have you ever silently complied with something against your faith because you feared the consequences? According to this lesson, what is the minimum requirement when you cannot openly resist?
?
Do you truly rely on Allah to suffice you from the harm of unjust authorities, or does fear control your actions? What is one concrete step you can take this week to strengthen your tawakkul in such situations?

Sins Other Than Kufr Do Not Justify Removing the Imam
Commentator explains that for sins other than kufr, the imam cannot be removed and is not automatically deposed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that only kufr (disbelief) can nullify the imam's contract; other sins do not permit removal or automatic deposition.

Core Idea
Only kufr removes the imam; no sin less than that breaks the pledge.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid addresses a critical question in Islamic political theology: when is it permissible to remove a ruler? The answer is very narrow. Only if the imam commits an act of kufr (disbelief) can he be removed. Other sins, even major ones, do not justify rebellion or deposition. This teaching guards against political instability and emphasizes the sanctity of the oath of allegiance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its literal translation
Read the Arabic text and the literal translation side by side. Notice the emphatic negation: removal is not permissible, and the imam is not deposed.
The passage quotes the poem line and then explains it.
2
Identify the exception
Find the phrase 'other than this' and understand that 'this' refers to kufr. The commentator specifies that only kufr, not fisq, justifies removal.
The condition 'without deeming it lawful' is also mentioned.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why this rule exists. The imam's authority is not based on personal righteousness alone; once the pledge is given, it remains binding unless the imam leaves Islam.
This principle is common in Sunni political thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِغَيرِ هَذا لَا يُبَاحُ صَرْفُهُ وَلَيسَ يُعْزَلَ انْ يَزُولَ وَصْفُهُ (بِغَيْرِ هَذَا الكُفر من جميع المعاصي إذا ارتكبها من غير استحلال (لَا يُبَاحُ) أي: لا يجوز (صَرْفُهُ) عن الإمامة وخلعه، لا سرًّا ولا جهرًا (وَلَيسَ يُعْزَلَ) الإمام ( أنْ يَزُولَ ) ، أي : إذا عُقدت البيعةُ لإمام عَدلٍ ثمَّ زالَ (وَصْفُهُ) السَّابق ، أعني العدالة ؛ بطرو الفسق ؛ فإنَّه لا ينعزل عند الله تعالى
Other than this, his removal is not permissible, and he is not deposed when his attribute departs. (Other than this kufr, from all sins if he commits them without deeming them lawful, it is not permissible) meaning it is not allowed (his removal) from the imamate and his deposition, neither secretly nor openly. (And the imam is not deposed when his attribute departs), i.e., if the pledge of allegiance was contracted for a just imam, then his previous attribute, namely justice, leaves due to the occurrence of fisq (sinfulness), then he is not deposed in the sight of Allah.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage clarifies the limits of permissible action against an imam. It begins by quoting the poem line: 'Other than this, his removal is not permissible, and he is not deposed when his attribute departs.' The commentator explains: 'Other than this' refers to kufr; any other sin, as long as the imam does not deem it lawful (istihlal), does not justify removal. It is not permissible to remove him from the imamate or to depose him, whether secretly or openly. Furthermore, the commentator addresses a subtle point: if the imam was just at the time of his election (his pledge of allegiance was contracted for a just imam), but later his attribute of justice departs due to fisq (sinfulness, e.g., starting to drink alcohol or commit major sins), he is not automatically deposed in the sight of Allah. That is, his authority remains valid, and rebellion against him is not allowed. The only exception, as stated previously, is if he commits kufr.

Key Terms
الفسق
العدالة
البيعة
العزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Major sins like zina or theft justify removal of the imam.
Correction: Only kufr nullifies the imamate; other sins do not permit removal or automatic deposition.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what happens to an imam who was initially just but later becomes sinful (fasiq)?
A) He is immediately deposed
B) He remains imam and cannot be removed
C) He must be removed secretly
D) The community must appoint a new imam

Key Takeaway
Only an act of kufr can remove the imam; other sins do not break the pledge of allegiance, and the imam remains in authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly wished for a leader to fall because of his personal sins, forgetting that only kufr can remove him? What does this reveal about your heart's attachment to power rather than obedience to Allah's command?
?
If you were in a position of authority and fell into a sin that you cannot justify, would you be tempted to resign or hide it? How does this lesson's ruling that you remain in authority until kufr affect your sense of responsibility before Allah?
?
The passage says the imam is not deposed 'in the sight of Allah' even if he becomes sinful. Are you willing to submit to an imperfect leader, trusting that Allah's decree placed him there, even when his actions trouble your conscience? What would that cost you personally?

Ruling on Obeying an Imam Who Commands Disbelief
Explains ruling on obeying an imam who commands kufr, including exception for fear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when it is permissible to disobey an imam who commands an act of disbelief, and how to deal with the oath of allegiance in such a situation.

Core Idea
When an imam commands kufr, obedience is forbidden—unless you fear death.

This lesson addresses one of the most sensitive issues in political theology: the limits of obedience to a Muslim ruler. The author explains that if the imam commands something that constitutes disbelief (kufr), the subject is not obliged to obey. However, there are important nuances regarding fear and the manner of renouncing allegiance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of the ruling.
Notice how the author distinguishes between explicit and implicit kufr.
2
Identify the exception clause
Find the condition that permits outward obedience: fear of death based on circumstantial evidence.
The exception is narrow and based on Quranic principle.
3
Understand renouncing allegiance
The passage describes two ways: openly if possible, secretly if not. Note the proof text from the Quran.
The goal is to eventually remove the imam when able.
4
Reflect on the proof text
Consider why the author cites the verse 'Allah has not given the disbelievers any way over the believers.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات
أو ضمني؛ فلا تجوز طاعته إلا إن خفت القتل بقرائن الأحوال، فإن لم تخف القتل وقدرت على طرح عهده (فانبذنَّ) أي: فاطرحنَّ عهده وبيعته جهرة لكفره الموجب لانخلاعه عن استحقاق التوفية له؛ إذ لم يجعل الله للكافرين على المؤمنين سبيلا، فإن لم تقدر على الجهر بذلك فاطرحه سرًّا حتى تجد قدرة القيام بخلعه (فالله يكفينا أذاه) أي الجائر الذي أمر بالكفر وتلبس به. (وحده) إذ هو الذي ناصيته بيد قدرته.
بغير هذا لا يباح صرفه وليس يعزل أن يزول وصفه.
(بغير هذا الكفر من جميع المعاصي إذا ارتكبها من غير استحلال لا يباح) أي: لا يجوز صرفه عن الإمامة وخلعه، لا سرًّا ولا جهرًا. (وليس يعزل) الإمام (أن يزول)، أي: إذا عقدت البيعة لإمام عدل ثم زال وصفه السابق، أعني العدالة؛ بطرو الفسق؛ فإنه لا ينعزل عند الله تعالى، وإن استحق العزل عند الناس، وأما إذا أمر بمحرم مختلف فيه كأكل الخيل أو لعب الشطرنج، أو أمر بمكروه أو مندوب أو مباح وجبت طاعته، فالأقسام ثلاثة.
Section on samiyyat. Or implicit (ḍimnī); so obedience to him is not permissible unless you fear death from circumstantial evidence. If you do not fear death and you are able to cast aside his covenant, then 'cast it' meaning: cast aside his covenant and his oath of allegiance openly, because of his disbelief which necessitates his forfeiture of the right to fulfillment; since Allah has not given the disbelievers any way over the believers. If you are not able to do so openly, then cast it aside secretly until you find ability to carry out his removal. 'Then Allah suffices us against his harm' meaning the unjust one who commanded disbelief and engaged in it. 'Alone' for He is the One whose forelock is in His power. Other than this, his removal is not permitted, and he is not removed when his attribute disappears. 'Other than this disbelief' from all sins, if he commits them without considering them lawful, 'is not permitted' meaning: it is not permissible to remove him from the imamate and depose him, secretly or openly. 'And he is not removed' the imam 'when his attribute disappears,' meaning: when the oath of allegiance is given to a just imam, then his previous attribute, namely justice, disappears due to the occurrence of fisq, then he is not removed in the sight of Allah, even if he deserves removal in the sight of people. And if he commands a disputed prohibition like eating horses or playing chess, or commands a disliked act, a recommended act, or a permissible act, obedience to him is obligatory. So the divisions are three.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a verse of the poem dealing with the imamate, specifically the conditions under which an imam may be disobeyed or removed. The author first addresses the case where the imam commands an act of disbelief (kufr), whether explicit or implicit. In such a case, obedience is forbidden unless the subject fears death, in which case he may obey outwardly while planning to cast off allegiance secretly. The proof text from the Qur'an is cited: 'Allah has not given the disbelievers any way over the believers.' The author then explains that for sins other than kufr (committed without istihlal), the imam cannot be deposed. Moreover, even if a just imam later becomes corrupt (fasiq), he is not automatically removed from office in Allah's sight, though he deserves removal from the community's perspective due to resulting harms. Finally, the author categorizes commands: if the imam commands something that is permissible, recommended, disliked, or even a disputed prohibition, obedience is obligatory; if he commands something clearly forbidden, it is not. Thus three categories emerge: commands of kufr (must be disobeyed), commands of other sins (not to be obeyed but imam not deposed), and commands of permissible or non-consensus prohibitions (obedience required).

Key Terms
سمعيات
ضمني
كفر
بيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any sin by the imam justifies disobedience.
Correction: Only commands of disbelief (kufr) justify disobedience; other sins do not.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what condition allows obeying an imam who commands disbelief?
A) When the imam is generally unjust.
B) When the subject fears death based on circumstantial evidence.
C) When the command is a disputed prohibition.
D) When the imam later becomes corrupt.

Key Takeaway
When an imam commands disbelief, obedience is forbidden; if fearing death, one may obey outwardly while renouncing allegiance secretly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever faced a situation where someone in authority asked you to do something that conflicted with your faith? Did you obey outwardly out of fear, and if so, did you still internally reject that command? This lesson teaches that such internal rejection (casting aside allegiance secretly) is required. Have you truly done that in your heart, or have you let the matter slide? What one step will you take today to realign your hidden loyalty solely to Allah?
?
The passage states that when an imam commands disbelief, he forfeits his right to fulfillment. Consider your own relationships—are there people in your life who demand obedience in matters that compromise your faith? Are you secretly honoring their authority when Allah commands you to renounce it? Be honest: have you been a coward or a true believer in your heart? What will you do to actively cast off any allegiance to those who lead you away from Allah?
?
The lesson concludes that Allah alone suffices against the harm of an unjust ruler. Do you truly rely on Allah for protection against those who oppress your faith, or do you cling to compromises fearing their harm? Are you willing to declare openly, if possible, that your allegiance is to Allah alone, even if it costs you comfort or safety? If you fear death, the lesson permits concealment—but does your concealment stem from genuine fear or from laziness and desire for ease? Examine your intentions now.

Ruling on Other Sins and the Status of a Fasiq Imam
Explains that sins other than kufr do not permit deposition, and outlines three categories of commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that an imam is not deposed for sins other than kufr, and the three categories of his commands.

Core Idea
Sins other than disbelief do not justify deposing an imam, and his orders must be obeyed unless they command a clearly prohibited act.

Having discussed the extreme case of kufr, the author now turns to lesser sins. He clarifies that an imam who commits sins without regarding them as lawful does not lose his position, even if he becomes corrupt. Moreover, the author outlines the three categories of commands an imam may issue, specifying when obedience is required.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and translation focusing on the rule for non-kufr sins.
Notice the phrase 'without considering them lawful' (istiḥlāl).
2
Understand the concept of fisq
The passage discusses an imam who becomes fasiq after being just. Note that he is not removed in Allah's sight, though he deserves removal from the community's perspective.
3
Learn the three categories
Identify the three divisions mentioned: kufr, other sins, and permissible/disputed commands.
Note that for disputed prohibitions and disliked acts, obedience is required.
4
Apply the rule
Consider how this ruling applies to real scenarios: what if the imam commands eating horses (disputed)? The answer is obedience.

The Ruling of Enjoining Good and Forbidding Evil as a Communal Obligation
The commentator explains the obligation and why the poet emphasized 'amr over nahy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-amr bil-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar is a fard kifayah and why the poet emphasized 'amr over nahy.

Core Idea
Enjoining good is a communal duty; commanding good implies forbidding evil.

After laying down the importance of the imamate, the poet turns to the practical pillar that upholds it: commanding good and forbidding evil. This passage clarifies the ruling (communal obligation) and explains why the poet explicitly mentioned 'amr and implicitly included nahy. Let's read the original text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the footnote markers (e.g., ۳, ٤) which indicate annotations.
The passage is concise but dense with meaning.
2
Identify the two key points
First, identify the ruling: it says 'وجوبًا كفائيا' (communal obligation). Second, understand why the poet only mentions 'amr and not nahy explicitly. The passage says it's because the command entails the prohibition and because 'amr is nobler.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ من الإمامة عقبها بما يتوقف القيام به غالبا عليها، وهو الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر ، فقال : (وَأْمُرْ بَعُرْفٍ) (۳) وَانْهَ عن مُنكَرٍ (٤) وجوبًا كِفائيَّا(٥) ، وإِنَّما تركَ النّهى عن المنكر لاستلزام الأمر له (٦) ، وآثَرَ الأمر لشرفه (۷) .
When he finished from the imamate, he followed it with what its establishment generally depends upon, and it is al-amr bil-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar. So he said: 'And command with good' (3) 'and forbid from evil' (4) as a communal obligation (5). He only left out the prohibition of evil because the command entails it (6), and he preferred the command due to its nobility (7).
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling of al-amr bil-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar as derived from the poem. The commentator states that after discussing the imamate, the poet addresses what is necessary for its proper establishment. The obligation is specified as fard kifayah (communal duty). The poet intentionally omitted the explicit mention of nahy 'an al-munkar because the command to do good logically entails the prohibition of its opposite. He preferred to mention 'amr because it is associated with praiseworthy actions.

Key Terms
الأمر بالمعروف
النهي عن المنكر
وجوب كفائي
استلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling of al-amr bil-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar according to this passage?
A) It is fard 'ayn
B) It is fard kifayah
C) It is sunnah
D) It is mubah

Key Takeaway
The obligation to enjoin good and forbid evil is a communal duty, and the command to enjoin good logically includes the prohibition of evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that enjoining good is a communal duty. But when was the last time you actually commanded someone to do good, not just silently disapproved? What holds you back, and will you act on this lesson today?
?
The commentator says commanding good is nobler than forbidding evil. Does your practice reflect that, or do you focus more on criticizing wrong? How can you shift your efforts toward promoting good?
?
Since this obligation is communal, what excuse have you been using to avoid your share? If everyone makes the same excuse, the duty collapses. Will you be among those who step forward?

Defining 'Urf and Munkar in Islamic Law
The commentator defines 'urf and munkar linguistically and in Islamic law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and shar'i definitions of 'urf (good) and munkar (evil) as used in kalam.

Core Idea
'Urf and munkar are not mere customs – they are defined by divine law and recognized by sound human nature.

In Islamic theology, the terms 'urf (good) and munkar (evil) are not left to subjective opinion. The commentator here clarifies their meanings both linguistically and within the framework of the Shari'ah, showing that they encompass all acts of obedience and kindness, as well as everything the Law recommends.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own wording come through before you interpret.
2
Analyze the definition of 'urf
Notice that 'urf is defined as 'a comprehensive name' for four categories: obedience to Allah, drawing near to Him, kindness to people, and everything the Law has recommended. This shows it is not limited to custom.
3
Understand munkar and the 'predominant attributes'
Munkar is simply the opposite. Both terms are described as 'al-sifat al-ghaliba' – originally adjectives that have become nouns. The explanation 'a matter known among people, when they see it they do not disapprove' highlights that these are recognized by sound human nature, as defined by scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعُرف لغة في (۱) المعروف (۲) ، وهو: اسم جامع لكلِّ ما عُرِف طاعة الله والتقرب إليه، والإحسان إلى النَّاس، وكلّ ما نَدبَ إليه الشَّرِعُ (۳) ، والمُنكر ضِدُّه ، وهو مِن الصَّفات الغالبة(٤) ، أي : أمرٌ معروفٌ بَيْنَ النَّاس إذا رأوه لا يُنكروه (ه)
And al-'urf linguistically is (1) al-ma'ruf (2), and it is a comprehensive name for everything that is known as obedience to Allah and drawing near to Him, and kindness to people, and everything that the Law has recommended (3). And al-munkar is its opposite, and it is among the predominant attributes (4), meaning: a matter known among people, when they see it they do not disapprove of it (5).
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage defines the key terms 'urf and munkar. 'Urf is synonymous with ma'ruf, and it encompasses all acts of obedience to Allah, acts of drawing near to Him, acts of kindness to people, and everything the Shari'ah has urged. Munkar is its opposite. Both terms are described as 'predominant attributes' (al-sifat al-ghaliba), meaning they are originally adjectives but have become used as nouns. The phrase 'a matter known among people such that when they see it they do not disapprove of it' explains why it is called ma'ruf: it is recognized and not rejected by sound human nature, specifically the scholars.

Key Terms
العرف
المعروف
المنكر
الصفات الغالبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: 'Urf is merely custom or tradition
Correction: 'Urf in this context is a comprehensive term for all acts of obedience, closeness, kindness, and legal recommendations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is included in the definition of 'urf (ma'ruf)?
A) Only acts of worship
B) Only acts of kindness
C) All acts of obedience, closeness to Allah, kindness to people, and everything the Law recommends
D) Whatever people customarily do

Key Takeaway
'Urf and munkar are defined by the Shari'ah as comprehensive categories encompassing all actions that are either recommended or disapproved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you evaluate your daily actions by whether they are ma'ruf (obedience to Allah, drawing near to Him, kindness) or by what is convenient or socially acceptable? What is one specific action you will change today to align with this definition?
?
The passage says ma'ruf includes 'kindness to people.' Is there a person you have ignored or treated poorly? What concrete act of kindness will you perform for them before the day ends, seeking Allah's pleasure?
?
Have you ever normalized a sin because 'everyone does it'? How will you remind yourself that munkar is defined by the Shari'ah, not by majority opinion? Write down one verse or hadith to memorize and recite when tempted by this excuse.

Proofs for Enjoining Good and Forbidding Evil
The commentator cites Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma' as evidence for the obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scriptural proofs for the obligation of enjoining good and forbidding evil, and the three levels of changing wrongdoing as outlined in the hadith.

Core Idea
Enjoining good and forbidding evil is proven by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

This lesson presents the foundational evidence that establishes the duty of commanding good and prohibiting evil. The commentator draws directly from the Quran, the Sunnah of the Prophet, and the consensus of scholars. By examining these proofs, we see that this obligation is not merely a recommendation but a firmly established duty with graded responsibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the complete Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the concise style and the flow from general claim to specific evidence.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage states that the obligation is proven by three sources: the Book (Quran), the Sunnah, and Ijma' (consensus). Locate each one in the text.
The Quranic verse and the hadith are examples, not the proofs themselves.
3
Understand the three levels
The hadith outlines three levels of changing an evil: with the hand, with the tongue, and with the heart. Note which is the weakest and what that implies for every believer.
Changing with the heart is a minimum duty, showing that even silent disapproval is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على وجوبهما بالشَّرع عندنا الكتاب والسُّنَّة والإجماع ، كقوله تعالى: وَلْتَكُن مِّنكُمْ أُمَّةٌ يَدْعُونَ إِلَى الْخَيْرِ وَيَأْمُرُونَ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٠٤] الآية ، ، وكحديث أبي سعيد الخدري له قال : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : مَن رَأى مِنكُمْ مُنكرًا فليُغيّره بيده (٦) ، فإنْ لم يَستَطِع فَبِلسَانِه ، فإِنْ لم يَستطِعْ فبقَلْبِه ، وذَلِكَ أَضْعَفُ الإيمانِ» (۷)(۸) .
And the proof for the obligation of both (al-amr and al-nahy) according to the Shari'ah with us is the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. Such as His statement, the Most High: 'And let there be from you a nation inviting to good, commanding with what is right...' (Quran 3:104) the verse, and the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, say: 'Whoever among you sees an evil, let him change it with his hand; if he is unable, then with his tongue; if he is unable, then with his heart, and that is the weakest of faith.' (6)(7)(8).
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage presents the scriptural evidence for the obligation of al-amr bil-ma'ruf wa al-nahy 'an al-munkar. The proofs are three: the Quran, the Sunnah, and consensus (ijma'). The commentator mentions the Quranic verse from Surah Al-Imran (3:104) which commands the existence of a group that calls to good and enjoins what is right. Then he cites the well-known hadith narrated by Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, which outlines three levels of changing evil: by hand (for those in authority), by tongue (for scholars and preachers), and by heart (for the general public, indicating disapproval). The hadith concludes that the last level is the weakest of faith, showing that even inner disapproval is a minimal requirement.

Key Terms
الدليل
الكتاب
السنة
الإجماع
تغيير المنكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that only those in authority can enjoin or forbid evil.
Correction: The hadith clearly obligates all believers to at least change with the heart, showing that even inner disapproval is a minimal requirement.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, what is the weakest level of faith?
A) Changing with the hand
B) Changing with the tongue
C) Changing with the heart
D) Not changing at all

Key Takeaway
The obligation of enjoining good and forbidding evil is firmly established by the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, with graded responsibilities for all believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a clear evil in your daily life, does the hadith's command to change it weigh on your conscience? What usually holds you back from at least speaking up with your tongue?
?
The Prophet described changing with the heart as the 'weakest of faith'. How does that label make you feel about your own faith when you remain silent? Are you content with the weakest level?
?
If you truly believed that your silent disapproval is still an obligation, how would it change the way you react to wrongs you see online, at work, or in your family? What is one specific step you will take this week to act with your hand or tongue?

Definition of al-Ma'ruf and al-Munkar
Definition of al-ma'ruf and al-munkar from linguistic and technical perspectives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of al-ma'ruf and al-munkar as used in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Al-ma'ruf is all that is known to be good; al-munkar is its opposite.

Before we can discuss the obligation of commanding right and forbidding wrong, we must first understand what these terms mean. The commentator begins by defining al-ma'ruf and al-munkar from both linguistic and technical perspectives, grounding them in the shared recognition of the community.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice how the definition unfolds.
2
Identify the comprehensive term
Look for the phrase 'اسم جامع' and see what it includes: obedience, nearness to Allah, kindness, and everything recommended by the Law.
This shows that al-ma'ruf is not merely custom but is defined by revelation.
3
Understand al-munkar as opposite
Notice that al-munkar is simply the opposite of al-ma'ruf, and it is described as 'من الصفات الغالبة' meaning a predominant description of what people recognize as wrong.
4
Reflect on the communal aspect
The phrase 'أمرٌ معروفٌ بَيْنَ النَّاس إذا رأوه لا يُنكروه' indicates that al-ma'ruf is something known and not denied by people. This connects to the idea of communal recognition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعُرف لغة في المعروف، وهو: اسم جامع لكلِّ ما عُرِف طاعة الله والتقرب إليه، والإحسان إلى النَّاس، وكلّ ما نَدبَ إليه الشَّرِعُ، والمُنكر ضِدُّه، وهو مِن الصَّفات الغالبة، أي : أمرٌ معروفٌ بَيْنَ النَّاس إذا رأوه لا يُنكروه. والدليل على وجوبهما بالشَّرع عندنا الكتاب والسُّنَّة والإجماع، كقوله تعالى: وَلْتَكُن مِّنكُمْ أُمَّةٌ يَدْعُونَ إِلَى الْخَيْرِ وَيَأْمُرُونَ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٠٤] الآية، وكحديث أبي سعيد الخدري [؟] قال : سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : مَن رَأى مِنكُمْ مُنكرًا فليُغيّره بيده، فإنْ لم يَستَطِع فَبِلسَانِه، فإِنْ لم يَستطِعْ فبقَلْبِه، وذَلِكَ أَضْعَفُ الإيمانِ.
And al-urf (custom) linguistically means al-maruf (the known), and it is a comprehensive term for everything that is known to be obedience to Allah and seeking nearness to Him, and kindness to people, and everything the Law has recommended, and al-munkar (the wrong) is its opposite, and it is from the predominant descriptions, that is, a matter known among the people that when they see it they do not deny it. And the evidence for their obligation by the Law with us is the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus, such as His saying the Exalted: 'Let there be from you a nation inviting to good, commanding with the recognized' (3:104) the verse, and like the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri [؟] who said: I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say: 'Whoever among you sees a wrong, let him change it with his hand, if he is not able then with his tongue, if he is not able then with his heart, and that is the weakest of faith.'
Source pages p. 611

Commentary
This passage defines the terms al-ma'ruf and al-munkar and then provides the scriptural proofs for the obligation of commanding the right and forbidding the wrong. The commentator first explains that al-'urf linguistically refers to al-ma'ruf, which is a comprehensive term covering all acts of obedience and kindness recommended by the Law. Al-munkar is its opposite. These are described as predominant descriptors indicating that they are widely recognized matters. Then the commentator states that the evidence for their obligation is the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, citing the verse from Al Imran (3:104) and the well-known hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri which outlines the three levels of intervention: by hand, by tongue, and by heart.

Key Terms
المعروف
المنكر
العُرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that al-ma'ruf is defined purely by customary practice (urf) without reference to revelation.
Correction: The commentator explicitly includes 'everything the Law has recommended' (كلّ ما نَدبَ إليه الشَّرِعُ) showing that revelation is the ultimate determinant, though custom may overlap.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is included in the definition of al-ma'ruf?
A) Only acts of worship
B) Everything that is customary among people
C) Obedience to Allah, seeking nearness to Him, kindness to people, and everything recommended by the Law
D) Only acts that are known by the majority

Key Takeaway
Al-ma'ruf encompasses all acts of obedience and kindness recognized by the Law; al-munkar is everything contrary to that.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When you see something wrong, do you call it al-munkar only if it's clearly sinful, or do you also recognize subtle forms like neglecting acts of kindness? What is one small munkar you have tolerated today that you must now change?

Proofs for the Obligation of Hisbah
Evidence from Quran, Sunnah, and consensus for the obligation of commanding right and forbidding wrong.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural sources that establish the obligation of commanding right and forbidding wrong according to Ahl al-Sunnah.

Core Idea
The obligation of hisbah is proven by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

Having defined the key terms, the commentator now provides the evidence for why commanding the right and forbidding the wrong is obligatory. He cites three sources of legislation—Quran, Sunnah, and consensus—and gives specific examples: a verse from Surah Al Imran and a famous hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three sources mentioned at the beginning.
2
Examine the Quranic verse
Look at the verse from Al Imran (3:104). It commands the existence of a group that invites to good and commands the right. Reflect on its implications for the obligation.
The verse shows the obligation is communal (fard kifaya).
3
Study the hadith
Read the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri carefully. It outlines three levels of intervention: hand, tongue, heart. Understand the progression.
The note '[؟]' in the Arabic indicates a possible textual issue; the meaning is clear.
4
Connect to consensus
The commentator includes consensus (ijma) as a third proof. Reflect on why consensus is considered a valid source here.
Consensus of the scholars confirms the obligation from Quran and Sunnah.

Conditions for Enjoining Good and Forbidding Evil
The commentator lists three conditions for commanding right and forbidding wrong and their rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three conditions required for commanding right and forbidding wrong, and how their absence affects the ruling.

Core Idea
Commanding right is not always obligatory; it depends on knowledge, safety, and likelihood of success.

The commentator now outlines the conditions that must be met for the duty of enjoining good and forbidding evil to be valid and binding. These conditions ensure that the action does not cause greater harm or become ineffective. Let's examine each condition and its ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the Source Passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Notice how the commentator structures the conditions and their consequences.
The passage is dense but clear; take your time.
2
Identify the Three Conditions
Find each condition mentioned: knowledge, safety from greater evil, and strong presumption of effectiveness. Note how each is defined.
The conditions are listed before 'fa' (so) in the Arabic.
3
Understand the Rulings
See how the absence of the first two conditions makes the action forbidden, while absence of the third removes obligation. Reflect on the wisdom behind these distinctions.
This prevents harmful or futile interventions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شروط الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر: شروط الأمر بالمعروف أن يكونَ الآمر عالما بما يأمر به وينهى عنه؛ فلا يحلُّ للجاهل بالحكم النَّهيُّ عمَّا يَراهُ، ولا الأمر به، وأنْ يأمنَ أنْ يؤدِّيَ إنكاره إلى مُنكَرٍ أكبر منه، كأن ينهى عن شُرب الخمر، فيؤول نهيه عنه إلى قتل النفس أو نحوه، وأن يغلب على ظنه أَنَّ إنكارَهُ المُنكر مزيل له وأنَّ أمَرَهُ بالمعروف مؤثر في تحصيله؛ فعدم الشَّرطين الأولين يوجبُ التَّحريم، وعدمُ الشَّرط الثَّالث يُسقط الوجوب.
Conditions of commanding the recognized and forbidding the unrecognized: Conditions of commanding the recognized are that the commander be knowledgeable of what he commands and forbids; so it is not permissible for the ignorant of the ruling to forbid what he sees, nor to command it; and that he be secure that his denial does not lead to a greater unrecognized matter, such as forbidding drinking wine, so that his forbidding leads to killing a soul or similar; and that he be strongly confident that his denial removes the unrecognized and that his commanding the recognized is effective in attaining it; so the absence of the first two conditions makes it forbidden, and the absence of the third condition removes the obligation.
Source pages p. 612

Commentary
This passage enumerates the conditions for the obligation of commanding right and forbidding wrong. The commentator states three conditions: First, the person must have knowledge of what he commands and forbids; an ignorant person may not interfere. Second, he must be secure that his action will not lead to a greater evil, such as causing loss of life. Third, he must have a strong presumption that his action will be effective, that the evil will actually be removed or the good attained. The ruling is that if the first two conditions are absent, it becomes forbidden to enjoin or forbid; if the third is absent, the obligation is removed.

الوجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Everyone is obligated to command right and forbid wrong, regardless of circumstances.
Correction: This passage clarifies that the obligation is conditional on knowledge, safety from greater evil, and likelihood of effectiveness. Without these, it may become forbidden or non-obligatory.

Checkpoint
When does commanding right become forbidden according to the commentator?
A) When the commander is ignorant and fears greater evil
B) When it is ineffective
C) When the commander is not a scholar
D) When the ruler forbids it

Key Takeaway
Enjoining good and forbidding evil requires knowledge, safety from greater harm, and effective effort; without these it may be forbidden or non-obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Think of a situation where you tried to correct someone but your effort had no effect. Did you feel that the obligation was still on you? After learning that the condition of effectiveness removes obligation, how does that affect your sense of responsibility?

The Three Levels of Forbidding Evil
The author lists the three levels of forbidding evil and explains the Quranic verse about personal responsibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three levels of forbidding evil and how to reconcile the obligation with the Quranic verse about personal responsibility.

Core Idea
Forbidding evil has three levels: hand, tongue, heart—each with its own ruling.

This passage from the section on 'samiyyat' (matters of revelation) presents the well-known classification of forbidding evil into three levels. It also addresses a potential misunderstanding from a Quranic verse that might seem to contradict this duty. The author explains that the verse does not negate the obligation but emphasizes individual responsibility after fulfilling one's duty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the meaning.
2
Identify the three levels
Locate the three levels mentioned: changing by hand, by speech, and by the heart. Note the order and the conditions for each.
The strongest is by hand, then speech, then heart.
3
Understand the verse explanation
Read how the author explains the Quranic verse 'upon you are your souls'. Notice that it does not cancel the duty but clarifies personal responsibility.
The key phrase is 'when you have done what you are tasked with'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات ومراتب الإنكار ثلاثة أقواها أن يغير بيده وهو واجب عينا فورا مع القدرة فإن لم يقدر على ذلك انتقل للتغيير بالقول وليكن أولا بالرفق واللين فإن عجز انتقل إلى الإنكار بالقلب وهي أضعفها ولا يشكل على هذه القاعدة قوله تعالى يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا عَلَيْكُمْ أَنفُسَكُمْ لَا يَضُرُّكُم مَّن ضَلَّ إِذَا اهْتَدَيْتُمْ لأن معناها إذا فعلتم ما كلفتم به لا يضركم تقصير غيركم
Section on matters of revelation. The levels of forbidding evil are three: the strongest is that he change it by hand, and it is an individual obligation immediately when able. If he is not able to do that, he moves to changing by speech, and let it be first with gentleness and softness. If he is unable, he moves to forbidding by the heart, and it is the weakest. And this principle is not problematic with the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'O you who have believed, upon you are your souls; those who go astray cannot harm you when you are guided,' because its meaning is: when you do what you have been tasked with, the shortcomings of others do not harm you.
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage presents the well-known classification of forbidding evil into three levels. It begins by stating the strongest level: physical intervention, which is an individual obligation when one is able. If unable, one proceeds to verbal intervention, starting with gentleness. If even that is impossible, one resorts to forbidding with the heart, which is the weakest. The author then addresses a potential objection from the Quranic verse [5:105] which might seem to negate the duty of forbidding evil. He explains that the verse means that if you fulfill your own duty (including commanding good and forbidding evil), the misguidance of others does not harm you; it does not eliminate the duty itself.

Misconception: Some people interpret the verse 'O you who believe, upon you are your souls' to mean that one should not concern oneself with the sins of others at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the verse means: when you have fulfilled your own duty (including forbidding evil), the shortcomings of others do not harm you. It does not cancel the duty itself.

Checkpoint

A) Forbidding by the heart
B) Forbidding by speech
C) Forbidding by hand

Key Takeaway
The obligation to forbid evil is graduated: start with the strongest possible means, and the Quranic verse does not exempt anyone from this duty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition and Ruling of Slander (Namima)
The author defines slander, states its prohibition, and explains exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of slander (namima), its default prohibition, and the exceptional circumstances where it becomes permitted or obligatory.

Core Idea
Slander is forbidden unless needed to prevent serious harm.

This lesson defines slander as transmitting speech in a way that causes corruption. While it is generally prohibited by consensus, there are exceptions when necessity demands it, such as warning someone about imminent harm. Understanding these boundaries is essential for proper practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style.
2
Understand the definition of slander
The passage defines slander as 'transmission of people's speech, some to others, in a manner of corruption.' Grasp the key elements: speech transfer and intent to cause harm.
3
Note the general ruling of prohibition
Slander is prohibited by consensus. The command 'avoid' indicates a personal obligation covering speech, action, hearing, belief, and practice.
4
Examine the exception and conditions
The prohibition is lifted when there is a genuine need, such as warning against harm. The passage gives examples and notes that it may become obligatory or recommended.
5
Reflect on practical application
Think about situations where reporting someone's words is allowed. This helps internalize the balance between avoiding harm and fulfilling communal duties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف النَّميمة وبيان تحريمها: ولمّا كان اجتناب الغيبة والنميمة داخلاً في الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر، عقبه بقوله: (وَاجْتَنِبْ نَمِيمَهُ) أي: انفر منها وتباعد عنها، والأمر فيه للوجوب العيني، والمراد من الاجتناب: ما يعم القول والفعل والسماع والاعتقاد والعمل. والنميمة: نقل كلام الناس بعضهم إلى بعض على وجه الإفساد، أي: على جهة يترتب عليها الإفساد بينهم، وهي محرمة إجماعاً ما لم تدع الحاجة إليها وإلا جازت، كما إذا أخبرك شخص أن إنساناً يريد الفتك بك أو بمالك أو بأهلك؛ فهذا ونحوه ليس بحرام، وقد يكون بعضه واجباً وبعضه مستحباً، كما صرح به النووي رحمه الله تعالى، والمذاهب متّفقة على أنها ...
Definition of slander (namima) and explanation of its prohibition: And since avoidance of backbiting (ghiba) and slander enters into commanding good and forbidding evil, he followed it with his statement: (And avoid his slander) i.e., flee from it and keep away from it, and the command is for personal obligation, and the intended meaning of avoidance is that which encompasses speech, action, hearing, belief, and practice. And slander: the transmission of people's speech, some to others, in a manner of corruption, i.e., in a way that results in corruption between them, and it is prohibited by consensus as long as need does not call for it; otherwise it is permitted, such as when a person informs you that a man intends to kill you or take your wealth or [harm] your family; so this and the like are not forbidden, and some of it may be obligatory and some recommended, as al-Nawawi, may Allah have mercy on him, stated, and the schools of thought are agreed that it...
Source pages p. 613

Commentary
This passage defines the sin of slander (namima) and explains its ruling within the broader framework of commanding good and forbidding evil. The author first connects avoidance of backbiting and slander to the duty of enjoining good and forbidding evil. He then explains the command in the verse 'And avoid his slander' (from the poem) as a personal obligation, meaning one must avoid slander in all forms: speech (e.g., repeating words), action (e.g., gestures indicating transmission), hearing (not listening to the slanderer), belief (not accepting the slander as true, since the slanderer is a sinner and a sinner's report is unreliable), and practice (not acting upon the transmitted information). The formal definition of slander is given as 'transmission of people's speech, some to others, in a manner of corruption'—i.e., in a way that causes harm and discord. The default ruling is that slander is unanimously prohibited. However, exceptions exist when there is a genuine need, such as warning someone about a person who intends to harm them in life, property, or family. In such cases, reporting is not only permissible but may be obligatory (if harm is certain) or recommended (if harm is uncertain). The author cites Imam al-Nawawi and notes that all schools agree on this exception. The passage cuts off at 'and the schools of thought are agreed that...' likely completing with 'it is forbidden' or 'it is allowed' in the original.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all forms of slander are absolutely forbidden without exception.
Correction: The passage clarifies that slander is allowed when necessary to prevent harm, such as warning someone about an attacker.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Slander is transmitting speech causing corruption; it is forbidden by consensus except when necessary to prevent harm, such as warning about an imminent threat.

?

The Definition and Ruling of Slander (Namima)
Definition and ruling of slander with its exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of namima, its default prohibition as an individual obligation, the exception for necessity, and its status as a major sin.

Core Idea
Slander is prohibited by default, but necessity can make it permissible or even obligatory.

This lesson from Ithaaf al-Mureed defines the sin of slander (namima) and clarifies when it is forbidden and when it is allowed. You will learn the precise definition, the universally agreed ruling, and the important exception for cases of necessity, supported by a hadith from the Two Sahihs.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and ruling
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note how the author first states the obligation to avoid slander, then defines it explicitly.
The definition is key: 'transmitting people's words to cause corruption'.
2
Understand the exception for need
Identify the condition that makes slander permissible: legitimate need. The passage gives the example of informing someone of a threat to life, property, or family.
This exception prevents harm and can even make the act obligatory.
3
Note the evidence from hadith
The passage concludes with the hadith that a slanderer will not enter Paradise, establishing slander as a major sin (kabirah). Reflect on the severity.
4
Apply the lesson to daily life
Consider situations where speaking about others could be slander or permissible. Distinguish between gossip and necessary warning.
The key is intention: does the speech aim to cause corruption or prevent harm?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف النَّميمة وبيان تحريمها
والأمر فيه للوجوب العيني، والمراد من الاجتناب : ما يَعمُّ القول والفعل
والنَّميمةُ : نقل كلامِ النَّاس بعضهم إلى بعض على وَجْهِ الإفساد. أي: على جهة يترتّب عليها الإفساد بينهم، وهي مُحرَّمة إجماعاً ما لم تَدْعُ الحاجة إليها وإلَّا جازتْ ، كما إذا أخبرك شخص أنَّ إنسانًا يريد الفتك بك أو بمالك أو بأهلك ؛ فهذا ونحوه ليس بحرام ، وقد يكون بعضه واجبًا وبعضه مُستحبَّا ، كما صرح به النووي رحمه الله تعالى ، والمذاهب مُتَّفقةٌ على أنها كبيرةٌ ؛ لحديث الصحيحين: «لا يدخل الجنَّة نَمَّام»
Definition of slander and clarification of its prohibition. And the command regarding it is for individual obligation, and the intended meaning of avoidance is that which encompasses speech and action. Slander is: transmitting people's words from one to another in a way that causes corruption. That is, in a manner that results in corruption between them. It is unanimously prohibited unless need arises, in which case it is permitted—as when a person informs you that someone intends to attack you, your property, or your family; this and the like are not prohibited. Some of it may be obligatory and some recommended, as al-Nawawi clarified, may Allah have mercy on him. The schools are agreed that it is a major sin, due to the hadith in the Two Sahihs: 'A slanderer will not enter Paradise.'
Source pages p. 614

Commentary
This passage defines slander (namima) and explains its ruling. It begins by stating that the command to avoid slander is an individual obligation (wajib 'ayni). The avoidance includes speech and action. Then it gives the definition: slander is transmitting people's words from one to another with the intent to cause corruption. It is unanimously prohibited unless there is a legitimate need, such as informing someone of a threat to life, property, or family. In such cases, it is permissible and may even become obligatory or recommended. The passage cites al-Nawawi to support the gradation of rulings. Finally, it affirms that the schools agree that slander is a major sin (kabirah), supported by the hadith that a slanderer will not enter Paradise.

Definition of Ghibah and Its Prohibition
Defines ghibah, lists its modes, and states its prohibition by consensus and Qur'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of ghibah (backbiting), its various forms according to Islamic law, and its prohibition based on the Qur'an and consensus.

Core Idea
Ghibah is any act that conveys a Muslim's deficiency, and it is a major sin.

Backbiting (ghibah) is a serious sin that damages community bonds. This lesson from the Jawharat al-Tawhid commentary defines ghibah precisely, clarifies that it includes not just speech but also writing and gestures, and establishes its prohibition through the Qur'an and consensus. Understanding this definition helps you identify and avoid this sin in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text once and then the English translation. Notice how the author begins with the hadith about the nammam (slanderer) and then defines ghibah. Pay attention to the comprehensive rule (ḍābiṭ) at the end.
The passage is dense but structured: hadith → definition → modes → rule → Qur'anic proof.
2
Identify the modes of ghibah
The passage states that ghibah can be done by speech, writing, or gestures (eye, hand, head). Think of examples for each mode from daily life.
The definition says 'mentioning a person with what he dislikes' – this includes any communication.
3
Understand the prohibition and its evidence
The passage mentions that ghibah is prohibited by consensus (ijma') and supports it with the Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Hujurat. Reflect on why backbiting is compared to eating dead brother's flesh.
The hadith at the start establishes that it is a major sin (kabirah).

Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage defines the sin of ghibah (backbiting) and establishes its prohibition. The author begins by noting that the section on sam‘iyyat (matters known through revelation) includes a major sin (kabirah). To support this, he cites the well-known hadith from al-Bukhari and Muslim that a slanderer (al-nammām) will not enter Paradise. This sets the gravity of the topic. Then he gives the heading 'Definition of backbiting and explanation of its prohibition. The commentary then explains the line '(And backbiting)' from the poem: it is obligatory for every legally responsible person to avoid backbiting. He defines ghibah as mentioning a person with something in him that he dislikes. The definition is broad: it includes not only speech but also writing, or any gesture such as pointing with the eye, hand, or head. Next, he gives a comprehensive rule (ḍābiṭ): anything that conveys to another person a deficiency of a Muslim is a prohibited ghibah, and this is by consensus (ijmā‘). To reinforce the prohibition, he quotes the Qur'anic verse in Surah al-Hujurat (49:12), which compares backbiting to eating the flesh of a dead brother, highlighting its repulsiveness and gravity.

Definition and Prohibition of Ghibah (Backbiting)
Defines ghibah and its prohibition, extends to listening and heart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic definition of ghibah, its prohibition, and that it extends beyond speech to include listening, writing, gestures, and even internal thoughts.

Core Idea
Backbiting is not only speech; it includes every action that conveys a Muslim's deficiency.

This passage from the matn establishes the comprehensive definition of backbiting (ghibah) and its severe prohibition. It explains that backbiting occurs not just by speaking, but also by writing, pointing, or even thinking ill of a person. The author cites a powerful Qur'anic verse and notes that the prohibition extends to listening and assenting. Let's examine the text carefully to understand the full scope of this sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

2

The definition is general, covering any means of conveying a deficiency.
3
List the prohibited actions
Note the actions mentioned: speaking (implied), listening, writing, pointing with eye/hand/head, and even heart (internal belief).
This shows the broad scope of the sin.
4
Understand the evidence
The Qur'anic verse (49:12) is cited as proof; reflect on the metaphor of eating dead flesh to grasp the severity.
The verse is a powerful deterrent.
5

The passage ends with 'وقد استثني من ذلك' signaling exceptions; these are not listed here but will be covered later.
This indicates that not all talk about others is forbidden.

Commentary

Six Exceptions to Backbiting (Ghibah)
Al-Jujri enumerates six exceptions to backbiting in a poetic couplet, with partial commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six exceptional situations where speaking about another's fault is permissible or obligatory, as outlined in al-Jujri's poem.

Core Idea
Not all mention of faults is backbiting; six cases are allowed or required for justice and counsel.

Al-Jujri composed a concise poem listing six exceptions to the general prohibition of backbiting. These exceptions allow or even require mentioning someone's fault for legitimate purposes like seeking justice, giving advice, or asking for a religious ruling. In this lesson, we will explore the poem and the explanations of the first four exceptions, noting that the commentary is incomplete for the last two.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Read the Arabic poem once without analysis, then read the word-for-word translation. Notice the structure: the poem lists six items in rapid succession.
The poem is like a checklist of exceptions to the rule of backbiting.
2
Identify the six exceptions
From the poem, extract the six key words: تظلّم, استعن, استفت, حذر, عرف, اذكرن فسق المجاهر. Each represents a situation where backbiting is not sinful.
These are the 'six places' mentioned in the commentary.
3
Study the first exception: تظلّم (seeking redress)
Read the commentary's explanation of تظلّم. It permits mentioning oppression when complaining to authorities. The example shows it is allowed to say 'So-and-so wronged me.'
This exception is rooted in the hadith 'Delay by the rich is oppression.'
4
Study the next three exceptions
For استعن, استفت, and حذر, read the commentary's examples (helping remove evil, seeking a fatwa, warning about a defect). Understand the conditions: the intention must be sincere, not just to slander.
The story of Hind and Abu Sufyan illustrates seeking a fatwa.
5
Note the missing exceptions
The commentary ends before explaining عرف (making known) and فسق المجاهر (open sinner). Recognize that these are valid exceptions but require further study from other sources.
The poem itself confirms they are valid, even if the commentary is incomplete.

Section on Sam'iyyat. What al-Jujri composed in his saying: 'You are not backbiting - repeat, and take it organized like pearls: seek justice, seek help, seek fatwa, warn, make known, and mention the transgression of the open sinner.' Then it is absolutely forbidden, and saying 'I saw with my own eyes' does not absolve it, and among what is excused is mere thought that does not reach certainty. His saying: (You are not) is a prepositional phrase related to (repeat), and the lam is for explanation or meaning 'from', and the counted thing is omitted, i.e., in six places, and (backbiting) is fronted object for (repeat), i.e., repeat backbiting of need and its measure. His saying: (organized like pearls) is a حال, i.e., take it while it is organized and being like pearls. His saying: (seek justice) i.e., mention the oppression of the one who wronged you before the ruler and others, so you say: So-and-so wronged me and took such-and-such, and in the hadith: 'Indeed the one with a right has a say', and in it: 'Delay by the rich is oppression.' His saying: (and seek help) for changing the objectionable and returning the sinner to the right path, so you say to someone you hope can remove the evil: So-and-so does such-and-such, so forbid him from it, on condition that you intend to reach removal of the evil, if you do not intend that, it is forbidden. His saying: (and seek fatwa) as if you say to the mufti: So-and-so wronged me, does he have the right to do that or not? And Hind bint Utbah said to the Prophet (PBUH): Abu Sufyan is a miserly man, he does not give me what suffices me and my children, shall I take from his wealth without his knowledge? He said: Take what suffices you and your children in a reasonable manner. And he did not forbid her from that. His saying: (warn) i.e., warn a person who intends to gather with someone who has a defect, as advice to him, for if
Source pages p. 617

Commentary
This passage presents six exceptions to the prohibition of backbiting, as composed by al-Jujri in a poetic form. It begins with the section heading 'Qism al-Sam'iyyat'. The poem lists six cases: seeking redress from oppression, seeking help to remove wrongdoing, seeking a fatwa, warning someone about another's defect, making known the actions of an open sinner, and mentioning the transgression of an open sinner. The commentary then explains each exception in detail. The first line of the poem, 'لست غيبةٌ كرر', means 'You are not backbiting' in six specific contexts, and the phrase 'كرر' (repeat) indicates that the rule is to be mentioned repeatedly. The second line emphasizes that these exceptions are organized like pearls. The explanation of 'تظلّم' (seeking redress) permits mentioning the oppressor's actions when complaining to a ruler or authority. The hadith about 'مطل الغني ظلم' is cited. 'استعن' (seeking help) allows describing a wrongdoer to someone who can remove the evil, provided the intention is to stop the wrongdoing. 'استفت' (seeking a fatwa) permits mentioning the actions of a person to a mufti to know the ruling, as in the story of Hind and Abu Sufyan. 'حذّر' (warning) permits warning someone who intends to associate with a person who has a defect, for the purpose of sincere advice. The commentary is cut off before explaining the last two exceptions.

Self-Admiration (ʿUjb): A Hidden Sickness of the Soul
Definition of ʿujb, its prohibition, and comparison with riyāʾ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of ʿujb, why it is forbidden, and how it differs from riyāʾ.

Core Idea
Self-admiration is forbidden because it magnifies the servant's act, not the Master.

This passage defines a spiritual disease that can corrupt worship without the worshipper realising it. It explains what self-admiration is, why it is a sin, and how it differs from the related sin of showing off. Understanding this distinction is crucial for safeguarding one's deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a general warning, then the definition of ʿujb, then its ruling and comparison with riyāʾ, and finally a list of similar sins.
The passage uses a rhymed prose pattern in Arabic.
2
Identify the definition of ʿujb
Find where ʿujb is defined as 'the servant seeing his worship and deeming it great'. Note the examples given (worshipper, scholar, obedient).
3
Note the difference between ʿujb and riyāʾ
Focus on the timing: ʿujb occurs after the act, riyāʾ during. This determines whether the act is invalidated.
This is a key point of the lesson.
4
Understand the reason for prohibition
The passage explains that ʿujb is bad manners with Allah; the servant should see his worship as small before Allah's greatness. Reflect on the Qur'anic verse cited.
5
Review the list of blameworthy traits
The passage ends with a list of sins similar to ʿujb. Read them and consider how each relates to the theme of self-admiration or pride.
This list reinforces the broader context of avoiding blameworthy traits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان داءِ العُجب وسِر تحريمه (وَخَصْلَةٍ) أي: ويجب عليك أن تجتنب خصلةٌ (ذَمِيمَة) أي: مذمومة كَالعُجْبِ والكِبْرِ وَدَاءِ الحَسَدِ وَكَالمِرَاءِ وَالجَدَلْ فَاعْتَمِدِ (كَالعُجْبِ) وهو : رؤيةُ العِبادة واستعظامُها مِن العبد، فهو معصيةٌ متعلقة بالعِبادة هذا التّعلُّق الخاص، كما يعجب العابد بعبادته ، والعالم بعلمه، والمطيع بطاعته ؛ فهذا حرام غير مُفسد للطَّاعة، لأنَّه يقعُ بعدها، بخلافِ الرِّياء فإنَّهُ يقعُ معها فيفسدُها، وإِنَّما حَرُمَ العُجْبُ ؛ تعالى، إذ لا ينبغي للعبد أن يستعظمَ ما يَتقرَّبُ به لسيده ، بل يستصغره بالنسبة إلى عظمة سيّده ، لا سيّما عظمته ، قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا قَدَرُوا اللهَ حَقَّ قَدْرِو [الأنعام: ۹۱] ، أي : ما عَظْمُوه حقّ تعظيمه . ومثلُ العُجْبِ الظُّلمُ، والبغي ، والحرابة ، والغش ، والخَديعة، والكذب لغير مصلحة شرعيَّة، وتركُ الصَّلاة، ومنعُ الزَّكاة، وعُقوق الوالدين .
Explanation of the disease of self-admiration and the secret of its prohibition. (And a blameworthy trait) i.e. you must avoid a blameworthy trait, such as self-admiration (ʿujb), pride, the disease of envy, argumentation, and disputation; so rely on that. (Such as self-admiration) which is: the servant seeing his worship and deeming it great. So it is a sin related to worship in this particular way, just as the worshipper admires his worship, the scholar his knowledge, and the obedient his obedience. So this is forbidden but does not invalidate the act of obedience, because it occurs after it, unlike showing off (riyāʾ) which occurs with it and invalidates it. And self-admiration is only forbidden because it is bad manners towards God Almighty, for the servant should not deem great that by which he draws near to his Master, but rather should deem it small relative to the greatness of his Master, especially His greatness. He said, exalted: "And they did not appraise God with true appraisal" (Q 6:91), i.e. they did not magnify Him as He deserves to be magnified. And similar to self-admiration are: oppression, tyranny, banditry, fraud, deception, lying for no legitimate reason, abandoning prayer, withholding zakāh, and disrespecting parents.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage defines the blameworthy trait of self-admiration (ʿujb) and explains its ruling. It begins by listing several blameworthy traits (khiṣāl dhamīma) including ʿujb, pride, envy, argumentation, and disputation. The focus then shifts to ʿujb. Its definition: 'seeing one's worship and deeming it great' — the worshiper views his act with self-importance. This is a sin (maʿṣiya) related specifically to worship. The commentator notes that ʿujb occurs after the act, so it does not invalidate the act itself, unlike showing off (riyāʾ) which occurs during the act and nullifies it. The reason for its prohibition is that it constitutes bad manners with God (sūʾ adab maʿa Allāh): the servant should not magnify his own worship when drawing near to God, but should see it as insignificant before God's majesty. A Qurʾānic verse (6:91) is cited to illustrate that people fail to magnify God as He deserves. Finally, the passage lists other blameworthy traits similar to ʿujb in being blameworthy: oppression, tyranny, banditry, fraud, deception, lying without a legitimate religious interest, abandoning prayer, withholding zakāh, and disrespecting parents.

العُجب
الرِّياء
ذَمِيمَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People often think that ʿujb (self-admiration) nullifies the act of worship like riyāʾ does.
Correction: The passage clarifies that ʿujb occurs after the act, so it does not invalidate it, unlike riyāʾ which occurs during the act and invalidates it.

Checkpoint
Why does self-admiration (ʿujb) not invalidate the act of worship according to this passage?
A) Because it is a minor sin.
B) Because it occurs after the act, unlike showing off.
C) Because it is not related to the act of worship.
D) Because it is a form of bad manners but not a sin.

Key Takeaway
ʿUjb is a sin of self-admiration that occurs after worship, does not invalidate the act, but is forbidden because it shows bad manners by magnifying oneself before Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself feeling proud of your worship or knowledge, secretly admiring your own devotion? Read the definition of ʿujb: 'the servant seeing his worship and deeming it great.' When was the last time you felt that way? What would it take for you to see your deeds as small before Allah's greatness? This week, commit to silently reminding yourself after every good deed: 'This is nothing compared to His majesty.'
?
The passage says ʿujb is 'bad manners towards God Almighty.' How do you treat the Master of the universe when you finish praying or giving charity? Do you walk away with a sense of accomplishment, or do you feel humbled that He allowed you to serve Him? Tonight, after your next prayer, pause and say: 'O Allah, I am a weak servant who falls short; accept this small offering.'
?
Contrast ʿujb with riyāʾ: one is hidden, the other is for show. Which one do you struggle with more? Could your secret self-admiration be feeding a need for validation? Ask yourself: Am I more concerned with how my deeds appear to others or with how they appear to Allah? Make a concrete plan to check your intention before each action this week.

The Prohibition of Self-Admiration (Ujb) and Similar Sins
The author defines 'ujb and lists similar major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what 'ujb (self-admiration) is, why it is prohibited, and which other sins resemble it in being blameworthy.

Core Idea
Self-admiration is bad manners toward God, and it resembles many other serious sins.

This lesson continues the discussion of sam'iyyat matters. The author identifies 'ujb as a grave sin because it involves magnifying one's own deeds before God. He then lists a set of sins that share the same blameworthy nature, from injustice to dishonesty. Understanding these sins helps us purify our hearts and actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic matn and its translation. Notice the author's definition of 'ujb and the listing of similar sins.
Pay attention to the Quranic verse cited.
2
Understand the prohibition of 'ujb
The author explains why 'ujb is forbidden: we should consider our deeds small before God's greatness. Reflect on the verse from Surah al-An'am.
3
Identify the list of similar sins
The passage lists several sins like dhulm, baghy, hirabah, etc. Note each one and consider its definition from the commentary.
These are all major sins that corrupt the soul.
4
Reflect on the connection
Why are these sins similar to 'ujb? The commentary explains they are all blameworthy inner traits. Think about how they relate to the soul's corruption.
This shows the importance of inner purification.
